Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0510010736450.19010@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Fri, 30 Sep 2005, Tony Schlemmer wrote:

> The FVS318 is a pretty decent firewall and I say this as someone that has
> used OpenBSD for a number of years now for a firewall at several sites.
> I've been using an FVS318 as a a no fuss replacement for my OpenBSD
> firewall when I need to take the system down for an upgrade of the version
> of OpenBSD on it. I've also worked at a startup and had my non-computer
> techincal boss put me on the stop at CompUSA for a firewall for our office.
> I picked the FVS318 since it had the ability to support up to 8 IPSec
> tunnels which was great since we had a few people working offsite.

   Thank you very much, Tony, for the insight on both the FVS318 and OpenBSD.
For six years I used floppyfw -- version 1.09 all that time -- on a box
sitting under the desk. Because it worked, the Netgear sat here unused for
about a year. One day, a couple of months ago, something died on the floppyfw
box. Once I figured out that was the problem, I uploaded the FVS318 config to
the box from where I had stored it on my notebook, plugged everything in, and
was back on the 'Net in an hour or so.

   The FVS318 is very chatty: not only does it mail me a log of every smtp,
udp, and tcp packet it saw over the previous 24 hours (boring plot, but a
huge cast of characters), it also sends me individual notices of rejected
attempts to scan ports for vulnerable openings. It's silent, small, and just
works as designed and intended. I'm very happy with it.

   If I had major state secrets and a ton of free time when I was sitting here
twiddling my thumbs, I would probably invest in an OpenBSD firewall. As you
so clearly pointed out, it seems to be a career to maintain and is not
justified here.

   On the other hand, getting rid of the rest of the spam that makes it
through the postfix and spamassassin filters is a different story. Sigh. So
much comes through over Friday night it must be an after-school job for kids
everywhere.

Rich

Subject: [PLUG] Brag:  dirvish to be used by OSU Open Source Labs

Yesterday, I joined the bus tour of the Open Source Labs at Oregon
State University in Corvallis.  I was pleased to learn that they 
are big fans of dirvish, use it on their personal machines, and are
transitioning the big servers to use dirvish for disk to disk backups.

Since they are the primary repository for Mozilla, Kernel.org, Drupal,
Gentoo, and a number of other major open source projects, I guess 
this is hitting the open source big time for dirvish (which I am
currently managing).  Even better, perhaps I can motivate somebody
down there with better software skills to take over the management
of dirvish.

Keith

On Thu, 2005-09-29 at 18:13 -0700, Richard C. Steffens wrote:
> Kurt Sussman wrote:
> 
> >>Is there a tool I can use at my end to try to pinpoint a communication 
> >>problem between me and a specific web site?
> > 
> > 
> > I like 'mtr' for figuring out where the delays are. 
> > 
> >   http://www.bitwizard.nl/mtr/
> 
> Derek Loree wrote:
> 
>  > tcptraceroute with the correct port specified.
> 
> Thank you both. I downloaded and installed mtr and ran it. I also ran 
> traceroute. No obvious problems show up, nor did I have any trouble with 
> the web site in question, so I'm guessing it was a problem that went away.
> 
traceroute and tcptraceroute are two different programs.  traceroute
uses ICMP to trace the route, tcptraceroute uses tcp and a specified
port to trace the route.  The later is handy if the something in the
route doesn't honor ICMP requests.

> Now that I have the tools (I had forgotten about traceroute!), I'll use 
> them if and when the problem returns.
> 
Good Luck,
-- 
Derek Loree

From galens at seitzassoc.com  Sun Oct  2 23:48:58 2005
From: galens at seitzassoc.com (Galen Seitz)
Date: Sun, 02 Oct 2005 23:48:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] rpm spec file macro expansion
Message-ID: <20051003064903.7B24E8F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>


I'm trying to build some cross tools for the AVR microcontrollers, and I
need to work around an issue with file collisions.  When I build
cross versions of binutils, gcc, and gdb, the info files that get created
have the same name and path as the corresponding files for the native
host tools.  In order to eliminate the conflict, I'd like to do something
like the following in my spec file:

%define _infodir %{_infodir}/avr

Due to the way macros are evaluated, this results in infinite recursion.
What is the correct way to accomplish this?  

I'm bit surprised that this issue with cross tool info file collisions
hasn't been addressed.  The actual executables aren't a problem.  They
are installed in two different places, neither of which causes a
conflict.  For instance, gcc gets installed as /usr/bin/avr-gcc and
/usr/avr/bin/gcc.  The man pages are also installed in a way that avoids
conflicts.  Only the info files seem to be a problem.

thanks,
galen


From heinlein at madboa.com  Mon Oct  3 09:32:09 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Mon, 3 Oct 2005 09:32:09 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] rpm spec file macro expansion
In-Reply-To: <20051003064903.7B24E8F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20051003064903.7B24E8F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.63.0510030927490.5378@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Sun, 2 Oct 2005, Galen Seitz wrote:

> I'm trying to build some cross tools for the AVR microcontrollers, 
> and I need to work around an issue with file collisions.  When I 
> build cross versions of binutils, gcc, and gdb, the info files that 
> get created have the same name and path as the corresponding files 
> for the native host tools.  In order to eliminate the conflict, I'd 
> like to do something like the following in my spec file:
>
> %define _infodir %{_infodir}/avr

Generally, I'd just a) specify your longer path throughout the spec 
file or b) define _infodir with a macro further up the chain or with a 
full pathname

a) When building and installing, specify --infodir=%{_infodir}/avr,
    and include that path in your %files section.

b) %define _infodir %{_datadir}/info/avr

    or

    %define _infodir /usr/share/info/avr

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

Subject: [PLUG] RAID for remote filesystems
In-Reply-To: <9e0a35780510031005k3cb56e10pe089883ade830a9c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <9e0a35780510031005k3cb56e10pe089883ade830a9c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <f4468cae0510031020i1aab57c0vff246c2727849ec1@mail.gmail.com>

Since your filesystem needs to be laid on TOP of a block-level device,
you can't really do what you're looking to... the closest
approximation would be using a cluster filesystem that takes into
account some underlying fabric connecting a bunch of block devices
(Lustre, PVFS, GFS, etc)...

Though it would likely be possible to hack together a bunch of network
block devices mounted locally and do this.  I'm not sure about your
sshfs or gmailfs filesystems, I'm guessing they won't enable you to do
that however.  Perhaps if you could kludge a sparse file as a block
device (cryptoloop or somesuch) accessed OVER these and mounted
locally you could do it?  If you're accessing these over commodity
internet with so many layers of abstraction, it's unlikely to be
usable or stable however :)

/eli

On 10/3/05, Matt Alexander <lowbassman at gmail.com> wrote:
> Does anyone know if it's possible to RAID remote filesystems? I'm thinking
> of being able to combine multiple sshfs or gmailfs filesystems into one
> mount point.
> Thanks,
> ~M
> _______________________________________________

> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Mon Oct  3 12:27:56 2005
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (slinkymax0r)
Date: Mon, 3 Oct 2005 12:27:56 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] RAID for remote filesystems
In-Reply-To: <9e0a35780510031005k3cb56e10pe089883ade830a9c@mail.gmail.com>
References: <9e0a35780510031005k3cb56e10pe089883ade830a9c@mail.gmail.com>
Message-ID: <41983.64.122.164.5.1128367676.squirrel@64.122.164.5>

> Does anyone know if it's possible to RAID remote filesystems? I'm thinking
> of being able to combine multiple sshfs or gmailfs filesystems into one
> mount point.

RAID is a block level or byte level technology... not a file level one. 
You could certainly mirror data between two systems, but it sounds like
you're more looking into representing multiple mount points as a single
name space.

Regards,
Max

From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Tue Oct  4 13:15:17 2005
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (slinkymax0r)
Date: Tue, 4 Oct 2005 13:15:17 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] RAID for remote filesystems
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.44.0510041053010.10088-100000@service.dwightandamy.com>
References: <41983.64.122.164.5.1128367676.squirrel@64.122.164.5>
	<Pine.LNX.4.44.0510041053010.10088-100000@service.dwightandamy.com>
Message-ID: <41558.64.122.164.5.1128456917.squirrel@64.122.164.5>

> On Mon, 3 Oct 2005, slinkymax0r wrote:
>
>> > Does anyone know if it's possible to RAID remote filesystems? I'm
>> thinking
>> > of being able to combine multiple sshfs or gmailfs filesystems into
>> one
>> > mount point.
>
> It sounds like you want to use a DRBD device.

This would require a seperate out of band network  (probably) gigabit
between google and the other block device.

Plus most of these places he wants to mount already have native
filesystems running on some sort of RAID, with the file systems being
exported via foo appilcation or protocol.  Rsync would be easier since
it's file level if you wanted simple mirroring, but that's impractical for
a number of reasons (no google shell account!)

You could probably do the same thing that drbd does using a native iscsi
driver and normal software RAID (or hardware).  No out of band or private
interconnect needed.

Regards,
Max


>
> From http://drbd.org
>
> What is DRBD
>
> DRBD is a block device which is designed to build high availability
> clusters. This is done by mirroring a whole block device via (a dedicated)
> network. You could see it as a network raid-1.
>
>



From danh at f2it.com  Tue Oct  4 14:16:09 2005
From: danh at f2it.com (Dan Haskell)

On 10/5/05, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org> wrote:
>
> Fedor Pikus wrote:
> > On 10/5/05, Roderick A. Anderson <raanders at acm.org> wrote:
> >
> >>I've looked and looked and can't find or come up with the correct
> >>combination of settings to get a "Fedora Core 4 - KDE" install from

> >>
> >>I Inherited system so I don't _really_ know what was done during the
> >>install.

> > I just uninstall the automounter, there is "autorun" for KDE, Gnome's
> one is
> > "magicdev" or some other piece of "magic". RPM can be uninstalled with
> no
> > other dependencies.
>
> Problem is I want my cake and to eat it too. There is also a DVD/CDROM
> in the system and I kind of like it automounting when I stick a DVD/CD
> into it.

Did some more looking and tried making changes to /etc/auto.misc to look
> at the correct device but still no joy.


auto.misc is from autofs, it's a different kind of automounter: autorun and
the like detect that a CD is inserted and mount it. autofs will intercept a
command like "ls /mnt/cdrom" and mount the CD if it's not already mounted.
autofs is configured through auto.misc, and you need to run autofs daemon.
You should not be using both automounters at the same time for the same
device and mount point.

autorun in KDE is next.
>
> Thanks for the clue-stick.
>
>
> Rod
> --
>
>

http://wild-light.com

> right click on the desktop ) that is part of my Fedora Core 1 install at
> home.

For Gnome in FC4, there is gnome-volume-manager.  The preferences are
set with Preferences, Removable Drives and Storage.  It has options
for mounting removeable drives, running commands for blank discs,
audio CDs, and video DVDs.

HAL and udev are doing most of the work; gnome-volume-manager is just
the policy piece.  My impression is that there is a beta KDE Volume
Manager which isn't included with FC4.
I have seen suggestion for running gnome-volume-manager with KDE.  I
don't know how to get this running or configured since I use Gnome.

 - Ian

Date: Wed, 5 Oct 2005 14:38:03 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] RAID5 suggestions
Message-ID: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>

I'm building a file server with 6 300GB SATA drives, using software
RAID5. This will probably be running Fedora Core 4. I want most of the
drive space available for file storage (/home/shares). Thing is, I know
you can't boot off a software RAID5 partition, just single or mirrored.
How would you folks set up your drives with redudancy and best use of
drive space?

Another question about SATA: How can I determine which physical drive
is /dev/sda, /dev/sdb, etc?

-Scott

From creswick at gmail.com  Wed Oct  5 14:51:38 2005
From: creswick at gmail.com (Rogan Creswick)
Date: Wed, 5 Oct 2005 14:51:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID5 suggestions
In-Reply-To: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <c834ec80510051451l35afb82asab745a2dcac6c49e@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/5/05, Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> wrote:
> How would you folks set up your drives with redudancy and best use of
> drive space?

A friend of mine just set up a raid-5 server using software raid with
one large partition on each disk (and a small, non-raided but manually
mirrored boot partition on each drive). He then used some combination
of lvm + xfs to repartition that huge raid partition.

End result is raid-5 with software, and he can dynamically resize
partitions (no remounting required).

If you want more details, I can put you two in contact..

--Rogan

From fpikus at gmail.com  Wed Oct  5 16:22:03 2005
From: fpikus at gmail.com (Fedor Pikus)
Date: Wed, 5 Oct 2005 18:22:03 -0500
Subject: [PLUG] RAID5 suggestions
In-Reply-To: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <a4fbdc7b0510051622k411ef6e2m7efb60c9d4e4530@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/5/05, Scott Van Hoosen <svanhoosen at yahoo.com> wrote:
>
> I'm building a file server with 6 300GB SATA drives, using software
> RAID5. This will probably be running Fedora Core 4. I want most of the
> drive space available for file storage (/home/shares). Thing is, I know
> you can't boot off a software RAID5 partition, just single or mirrored.
> How would you folks set up your drives with redudancy and best use of
> drive space?


You can set up /boot on RAID0 and / on RAID5 if you want, it's a bit tricky
(GRUB installer will not configure GRUB to boot, your system will hang on
boot after install, you'll need a boot CD and run GRUB manually to get it
properly installed) but it can be done. Then you can cut a small partition
from 2 big disks for /boot RAID0 and the same size small partition from
remaining disks to use for swap.

Another question about SATA: How can I determine which physical drive
> is /dev/sda, /dev/sdb, etc?


You just have to know which connector on the motherboard is SATA1 etc.

-Scott

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Oct  5 17:52:14 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 05 Oct 2005 17:52:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID5 suggestions
In-Reply-To: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <1128559934.20732.56.camel@denk.nakedape.cc>

On Wed, 2005-10-05 at 14:38 -0700, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> I'm building a file server with 6 300GB SATA drives, using software
> RAID5. This will probably be running Fedora Core 4. I want most of the
> drive space available for file storage (/home/shares). Thing is, I know
> you can't boot off a software RAID5 partition, just single or mirrored.
> How would you folks set up your drives with redudancy and best use of
> drive space?

I wouldn't use RAID5 is what I'd do.  While I've read plenty about
RAID5, there are certain aspects that remain elusive to me, so I tend to
prefer RAID1 and RAID0, which are simple and clear.

I'd peel off about 100MB of a couple drives for RAID1 /boot (or, with
300GB drives, do on all 6 and have a 6-way mirror).  Then I'd take out
1/2 or 1/4 of your RAM from each drive for swap space and set them all
at the same priority (the kernel with balance the swapping between them,
in a RAID0-like fashion, except if you lose some of them it will use
what's available, unlike a real RAID0, which would prevent you from
using any of it).

Partition the rest of the space on each drive as one partition and then
you could do several things.  Exactly how or what depend on the
application, but you could:
  o Use RAID5 on all 6 partitions
  o Make 3 RAID1 volumes and stripe (RAID0) across them--this is the
best performance, although the available space is less than RAID5.  This
is called RAID0+1 or 1+0; I've never actually been clear which.  You can
also make two RAID0 stripes and then mirror them with RAID1.
  o Make 3 RAID1 pairs and use them as you see fit; for example, the
first pair could be for your regular system stuff, then you could do
RAID0 with the remaining 2 RAID1 volumes.  Or you could do RAID5 with
the remaining 4 volumes.  You could also keep them partially independent
of each other if you're dealing with database applications and a DBA who
like to control what data goes on what spindles.

At any rate, with whatever scheme you choose, use LVM to allocate your
actual file systems.  In the latter case of two RAID1 volumes and a
picky DBA, for example, you can specifically allocate certain logical
volumes to certain physical volumes.  With FC4, you can even resize
ext2/3 filesystems on-line (while mounted), so it's even easier
(although I wish they'd included e2fsadm, which is easier than using
lvextend+ext2online, partly because I find myself continuously
forgetting the name of the latter command).  The LVM HOWTO gives you a
pretty good introduction.

Wil
-- 
Wil Cooley                                 wcooley at nakedape.cc
Naked Ape Consulting                        http://nakedape.cc
* * * * Linux, UNIX, Networking and Security Solutions * * * *

From 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com  Wed Oct  5 19:29:41 2005
From: 1s7k8uhcd001 at sneakemail.com (Steve Bonds)
Date: Wed, 5 Oct 2005 19:29:41 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID5 suggestions
In-Reply-To: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <20051005213803.62087.qmail@web52605.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <13279-84673@sneakemail.com>

On 10/5/05, Scott Van Hoosen wrote:
> I'm building a file server with 6 300GB SATA drives, using software
> RAID5. This will probably be running Fedora Core 4. I want most of the
> drive space available for file storage (/home/shares). Thing is, I know
> you can't boot off a software RAID5 partition, just single or mirrored.
> How would you folks set up your drives with redudancy and best use of
> drive space?

After a few misadventures with RAID-5 booting issues I've finally gone
to RAID-1 (mirroring) on all my systems.  Disks are cheap enough now
that RAID-1 on SATA makes good economic sense.

> Another question about SATA: How can I determine which physical drive
> is /dev/sda, /dev/sdb, etc?

This depends on the SATA channel each drive is plugged into.  SATA0 =
sda, SATA1 = sdb, etc.

  -- Steve

From blitters at gmail.com  Thu Oct  6 10:57:21 2005
From: blitters at gmail.com (Steve D...)
Date: Thu, 6 Oct 2005 10:57:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Troublesome linux box
In-Reply-To: <1128619620.5530.6.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>
References: <1128619620.5530.6.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>
Message-ID: <ff01ea180510061057k474e8012r7e5a2def7821911a@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/6/05, Sean Whitney <sean.whitney at gmail.com> wrote:
>
> The only clue I have is some messages from dmesg.  If anyone has any
> insight into some troubleshooting steps I'm really looking for some
> answers.  Please see the dmesg below....

  Your box is getting kernel oops messages.  They need to be decoded
using the copy of "ksymoops" for your kernel.

  These look like good oops messages.  They haven't been tainted.  It
looks like you may be hitting the same bug over and over again.

  It's also possible that it's *not* a kernel problem.  It could be a
BIOS or hardware issue, depending on which module the oops is
occuring.

See:
# How do I capture an Oops?
The linux-kernel mailing list FAQ
http://www.tux.org/lkml/#s4-3

Reporting bugs for the Linux kernel
http://www.kernel.org/pub/linux/docs/lkml/reporting-bugs.html

Steve D...

From griffint at pobox.com  Thu Oct  6 22:00:21 2005
From: griffint at pobox.com (Terry Griffin)
Date: Thu, 6 Oct 2005 22:00:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] where TF are Red Hat rpms?
In-Reply-To: <200510061914.00086.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200510061914.00086.carla@bratgrrl.com>
Message-ID: <200510062200.21711.griffint@pobox.com>

On Thursday 06 October 2005 7:13 pm, Carla Schroder wrote:
> I go to https://www.redhat.com/apps/download/advanced_search.html to
> download kernel sources. I search for "kernel-source" and "kernel". I get
> zero hits, and a stoopid message:
>
> "no hits on "kernel" (term was not used in search)"
>
> Cookies are enabled. sooo... is there a sekkrit incantation?

They don't ship a kernel-source-XXX.i386.rpm file any longer for both
RHEL and Fedora Core. They only ship kernel-XXX.src.rpm. For RHEL4 you
can find the source RPM here:

  ftp://ftp.redhat.com/pub/redhat/linux/enterprise/4/en/os/i386/SRPMS/

And for FC4 it's in this directory:

  http://download.fedora.redhat.com/pub/fedora/linux/core/4/SRPMS/

If you really want the kernel-source-XXX.i386.rpm file, it will
be created along with all the various binary kernels if you do an
'rpmbuild --rebuild' on the source RPM. 

Terry

The python module pyserial (http://pyserial.sourceforge.net/) makes it
relatively easy to create scripts that interact with serial ports.  I've
done this on a couple of occasions to talk to random hardware.  Another
possibility would be to use kermit's scripting capability.

galen

Subject: [PLUG] Copying a file to a network address rather than a device 
In-Reply-To: Your message of "Fri, 07 Oct 2005 08:16:52 PDT."
	<Pine.LNX.4.62.0510070812520.28087@salmo.appl-ecosys.com> 
Message-ID: <20051007161048.5C4328F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>

Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:

>    I need to copy firmware upgrades to one of my printers. It's the only way
> to do this under linux. The suggested syntax is 'cat <filename> > /dev/lp1'.
> However, the printers are not directly connected to the server, but are
> network nodes served by JetDirect cards.
> 
>    In /etc/cups/printers.conf, the printer needing the upgrade is defined as:
> DeviceURI socket://colorp:9100; it's network address is: 192.168.55.194.
> 
>    Can I redirect the 'cat <filename>' to the IP address rather than the
> device and end up with the same results?

Do you have a raw print queue, that is, one without any filters?  You
might be able to just 'print' the file.  If not, the command 'nc' may do
what you want.  Something like this might work:

nc 192.168.55.194 9100 < file


galen

> Russ Johnson wrote:

> > rhn.redhat.com and find it there.

> rpm.pbone.net is another good source of RPMs.
> 
> If you need to build kernel modules, the required header files, etc., 
> are in the kernel-devel package.

If you are needing to make patches to the kernel, you need to download the 
src.rpm for the kernel and build a new set of kernel rpms.  (I have to do 
this on my laptop to get cardbus to work.)

It is not too terribly hard to do if you understand how to build RPMs.  

A good book on this is:

Red Hat RPM Guide by Eric Foster-Johnson (ISBN: 0764549650) Redhat Press

Subject: [PLUG] where TF are Red Hat rpms?

On Thursday 06 October 2005 10:00 pm, Terry Griffin wrote:
> On Thursday 06 October 2005 7:13 pm, Carla Schroder wrote:
> > I go to https://www.redhat.com/apps/download/advanced_search.html to
> > download kernel sources. I search for "kernel-source" and "kernel". I get
> > zero hits, and a stoopid message:
> >
> > "no hits on "kernel" (term was not used in search)"
> >
> > Cookies are enabled. sooo... is there a sekkrit incantation?
>
> They don't ship a kernel-source-XXX.i386.rpm file any longer for both
> RHEL and Fedora Core. They only ship kernel-XXX.src.rpm. For RHEL4 you
> can find the source RPM here:
>
>   ftp://ftp.redhat.com/pub/redhat/linux/enterprise/4/en/os/i386/SRPMS/
>
> And for FC4 it's in this directory:
>
>   http://download.fedora.redhat.com/pub/fedora/linux/core/4/SRPMS/
>
> If you really want the kernel-source-XXX.i386.rpm file, it will
> be created along with all the various binary kernels if you do an
> 'rpmbuild --rebuild' on the source RPM.


Thanks, that's the magic URL.

For anyone who is interested, rpmfind.net does not have RPMs for RHEL4, and 
rpm.pbone.net has a few src.rpms. If you cruise through 
ftp://ftp.redhat.com/pub/redhat/linux/enterprise you'll see a lot of empty 
directories. I guess they want folks to use Red Hat Network. I don't have a 
retail copy, I just wanted to see inside some RPMs.

afaik, Red Hat doesn't publicly distribute binary packages for RHEL, 
though it does distribute source packages.

If you specifically need RHEL binary packages, you pretty much need a 
subscription. You're free to look at the RHEL source packages or the 
binary packages of the free-rebuild distros like CentOS.

From mike at mommabears.com  Fri Oct  7 10:26:31 2005
From: mike at mommabears.com (MJang)
Date: Fri, 07 Oct 2005 10:26:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] where TF are Red Hat rpms?
In-Reply-To: <200510070933.48336.carla@bratgrrl.com>
References: <200510061914.00086.carla@bratgrrl.com>

Message-ID: <1128705991.4043.12.camel@localhost>

On Fri, 2005-10-07 at 09:33 -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> Thanks, that's the magic URL.
> 
> For anyone who is interested, rpmfind.net does not have RPMs for RHEL4, and 
> rpm.pbone.net has a few src.rpms. If you cruise through 
> ftp://ftp.redhat.com/pub/redhat/linux/enterprise you'll see a lot of empty 
> directories. I guess they want folks to use Red Hat Network. I don't have a 
> retail copy, I just wanted to see inside some RPMs.

Red Hat does make a "30 day trial" edition of RHEL 4 available via
http://www.redhat.com/software/rhel/eval/ , which last I checked, does
include the binary RPMs on ISOs that you can burn and use for
installation. I think the 30 day limit is on the access period from the
RHEL 4 distro to the Red Hat Network.

They get away with it, because the GPL only requires them to release the
source code. Red Hat gets to do what they want with the binaries.

While folks like cAos (CentOS) and WhiteBox Linux have done an excellent
job with their rebuilds, they've had to use other tools (not included
with RHEL 4) to build the RHEL 4 SRPMs into their rebuild distributions.

Unfortunately, that means you can't mix and match some RPMs between RHEL
4 and the rebuilds, such as the Kernel source.

Thanks,
Mike

On Thu, 2005-10-06 at 19:13 -0700, Carla Schroder wrote:
> I go to https://www.redhat.com/apps/download/advanced_search.html to download 
> kernel sources. I search for "kernel-source" and "kernel". I get zero hits, 
> and a stoopid message:
> 
> "no hits on "kernel" (term was not used in search)"
> 
> Cookies are enabled. sooo... is there a sekkrit incantation?

Yes.

It is not included in Fedora Core 4.  If you need to build modules, you
need to install kernel-devel.  If you have to patch it, you need to drag
down the src.rpm and patch and rebuild the rpms.

mirrors.kernel.org:/fedora/core/4/SRPMS (or something like that) should
have what you want. That is from memory, so it is probably wrong.  (I am
dealing with really crappy wireless at lunch today.)

technical folks.

Yes ... Quantian is very cool indeed. The most recent versions have been 
on DVDs; if you want a CD-sized Quantian you need to go back a few releases.

One more Quantian goodie: if you have a room full of machines, you can 
boot Quantian up on all of them and have a cluster ready to run! I call 
it a Supercomputer on a DVD. :)

Yah, I caught that later, but left it in to see if people were paying
attention.  Since you are, I will fix it now, along with Piet's 
suggested reformmatting.

> My preference would be to drop the photo and just have Tux with the
> umbrella.  I think the image will be so small on a business card that
> it won't add anything to the card, but that's just MHO.  It's your
> idea and project, and more power to you for doing it.

I could go either way, but my intent is to show that we are an
organization (so to speak) of real people.  Some of the most
powerful images in computing and in corporate history are group
photos of the small teams that later grew huge;  Bill Gates and
the first Redmond team, Edwin Land and the founding Polaroid team,
the Mercury astronauts.  If we do what we hope to do, and PLUG
becomes what it could become, these cards may be worth a lot
of money as collectors items someday.  What, me hubris?

Perhaps that is overly ambitious, and a simple Tux with Umbrella
would be more restrained.

Keith

From plug at merlot.com  Fri Oct  7 22:38:17 2005
From: plug at merlot.com (Kurt Sussman)
Date: Fri, 7 Oct 2005 22:38:17 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Asterisk hints?
Message-ID: <20051008053817.GB9439@merlot.com>

I found out that my nice little PBX appliance, a 3 year old Talkswitch
24, can't be adjusted to work with the impedance of my Packet8 ATA, so I
get a nasty echo when it forwards calls to remote extensions. Talkswitch
support says that it's a hardware issue, and my box is too old to
upgrade to the newer hardware. For the $700 that a new Talkswitch will
cost, I should be able to build a perfectly adequate (or better)
Asterisk box, right?

I don't want to spend a pile of money or time on this, though I think it
will be a fun project and good for some quality geeking time.
Asterisk at Home looks like the way to go; a nice GUI, usable defaults,
etc.

I currently have one analog line and one VoIP line. If I can get a good
deal on raw VoIP lines I could use two of those, but I'm keeping one
analog line for emergencies. I want to continue to use three analog
phone devices: a Polycom speakerphone, a Panasonic cordless, and my fax
machine, an HP all-in-one. I'm not grokking the FXO/FXS distinction...
is there a link that explains those TLAs in terms of phone devices and
phone lines?

The Talkswitch is only a little bigger than my broadband router, and
handles 2 phone lines, 4 local extensions, 8 remote extensions, and 22
voicemail boxes. It has no fan, and makes noise only when it's ringing a
local extension (a little relay-like clicking). I like that a lot. So if
I'm going to build an Asterisk box, I want it to be as close to silent
and as frugal on power as possible. A very small footprint would be
great.

Any suggestions? Favorite links? Any and all advice is welcome.

Thanks!

--Kurt

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Fri Oct  7 23:45:31 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Fri, 7 Oct 2005 23:45:31 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Looking for new laptop vendor
Message-ID: <20051008064531.GA6122@gate.kl-ic.com>


I *used to* be quite a fan of IBM Thinkpads, because they *used to*
have quick and excellent repair service.  However, the sale of the
Thinkpad division to Levono has pretty much completed the destruction
of their quality and their reputation, so I am looking for a new
source of laptops.

I desire three things from a laptop vendor:

1)  Demonstrated excellent repair service.  Laptops are subject to
a lot of stress, and *will fail* - I want my next laptop to come 
from a company with a demonstrated history of quick repairs with no
nonsense.  As I said, that used to be IBM.  Who does it now?  Has
anyone here got any positive factory service stories to share?

2)  Mostly Linux compatable.  I want the display and the ethernet
and the mouse-surrogate to work.  I don't need working built-in
wifi.  For software compatability reasons, I need Intel architecture.

3)  I strongly prefer the IBM-style trackpoint to touchpads (Easier
to do precision work in jittery environments).  If there are other
stable pointing devices that work adequately, I would like to hear
about those, too.

I want compute speed (2GHz), don't care too much about graphics
speed, don't care at all about audio, games, etc.  While high 
reliability would be nice, it is impossible to evaluate, as the
machines with reliability track records are all obsolete, and the
various brandname sources all use the same manufacturers anyway.

Any suggestions?

Keith

PS:  My rant about IBM/Levono/Solectron's lousy performance can
be found at http://www.keithl.com/ibm.html .  I won't inflict it
on the mailing list.

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Sat Oct  8 00:10:37 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Sat, 8 Oct 2005 00:10:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Two screen presentation software
Message-ID: <20051008071037.GA6460@gate.kl-ic.com>


Thursday morning I attended an AEA event, a "mock VC" presentation.
My friend Jeff Zurshmeide and a partner did a mock presentation of
their imaginary startup to a committee of three local venture 
capitalists, followed by their evaluation meeting.  We got to see
how they perform the process;  very educational.  However, that is 
not what I am writing about here.

One of the things I observed was the relentless sequential nature of a
typical Powerpoint slide show performance;  Jeff and his friend had
their pitch rehearsed, and found it very difficult to change order
in order to answer the VC's questions.  I think some of that is the
paradigm imposed on them by their software, which does not easily
support skipping around.

Most modern laptops have two complete video generation systems;  it
is possible to display one image on the laptop screen,  and send an
entirely different image out the VGA/DVI port to the projector.  A
next-generation presentation program could make good use of this
capability.

This means the presenter can be looking at a "control panel", with
perhaps a quarter of the laptop screen devoted to the projected
image, and the other three quarters for script, messaging, notes,
slide sorter, etc.  Further, for situations where audience input 
redirects the order of the slides, the software should adapt to that,
allowing the presenter to resequence in real time, not touching on 
the out-of-order slide again, perhaps adding backup slides or removing
slides from the presentation in real time.   Slides could be made with
"hotspots", indicated areas on the user's panel that cause some 
animation (like text lining up, or movement in part of a diagram)
to happen on the projected screen.  Imagine a flowchart or data flow
diagram, where you can make "data" appear to move down the path you
are talking about by moving your mouse over it on your control panel.

I don't know of any presentation software that offers these capabilities,
but it might not be difficult to do under Linux.  Is there anything
like this out there?  If not, is anyone inspired to do this as an
open source project?   

Keith

-- 
Keith Lofstrom          keithl at keithl.com         Voice (503)-520-1993
KLIC --- Keith Lofstrom Integrated Circuits --- "Your Ideas in Silicon"
Design Contracting in Bipolar and CMOS - Analog, Digital, and Scan ICs

# from Keith Lofstrom
# on Saturday 08 October 2005 12:10 am:

>I don't know of any presentation software that offers these
> capabilities, but it might not be difficult to do under Linux. ?Is
> there anything like this out there? ?If not, is anyone inspired to do

My first thought on "how to do it today" is to just have a second X 
configuration with two displays and run the presentation software on 
the vga-out display.  If you configure without xinerama, the fullscreen 
should still work correctly (maybe even with xinerama given the right 
WM/software compatibility.)  You just move your mouse off of the edge 
of one desktop to control the other.  It's not going to do resequencing 
for you, but it would give you a desktop full of notes without 
disrupting the slide show.

Keith,

I recently bought a laptop from Dell.  Like you I researched the current 
status of vendors before I sent my hard earned money off. Dell's 
reputation is slipping, no doubt about it.  I've never had a bad 
experience with them and this sales call was no exception but my boss 
recently bought one of theirs and the comedy of errors that followed 
didn't make Dell look very good.  IBM's reputation seems to have 
slipped.  The one contact I had who had dealt with them recently (just 
pre-Lenovo sale) won't be talking to them again..  No one I talked to 
would consider any HP outside of a server.  Amazing unanimity on that 
point.  And really surprisingly Gateway came on strong in Laptops.  The 
same person didn't have much good to say about their desktops but 
strongly recommended their laptops, both in quality and service.  
Personally Gateway is on my "Burn me once" list but this person is quite 
knowledgeable, did her research, and loves her Gateway.

Dell has been my company of choice for quite a while now.  I'm typing 
this on an old Dimensions 850 I bought off their refurbished list 5 
years or so ago.  Both personally and professionally they've done well 
by me.  And at least on the Debian mailing lists there seem to be a fair 
number of people running Linux on Dells.

For a better index of Linux affinity, well you might want to look at:
http://www.linux-laptop.net/

And remember if you get the laptop today and don't plan on converting it 
for two or three years then the picture might look quite different by 
then.  You might try booting prospective laptops with Knoppix to see how 
it does recognizing the hardware (as well as giving the sales clerk a 
heart attack!).

A couple of pieces of advice I received on components: Fast laptop 
drives are expensive and you're not going to notice the difference for 
most applications.  Video editing was repeatedly mentioned as the 
exception to this.  Similarly faster processors simply don't earn their 
keep.  Look around 1.7 gig.  Faster processors chew up batteries and 
generate heat without speeding up things noticeably.  And again, there's 
quite a price jump.  I have no direct experience with this so I'm 
relying upon advice.  If anyone knows I'm full of it please don't be shy 
about saying so. Preferably before my 1.7 gig laptop with the big honkin 
but slow drive arrives! ;-) 

I like the TrackPoint as well.  I also find that I really appreciate the 
biggest, clearest screen I can get.  To me that's worth putting money 
into.  I don't play many games (okay, freecell) these days but I find I 
do like music and the idea of being able to take mine with me appeals to 
me. And I'm hearing that a DVD burner is the new "gotta have" as well as 
the memory key.  Think about BlueTooth for connecting to peripherals.  
Again, no direct experience with these, just passing on the advice I 
thought was good.

Good luck and I wouldn't mind hearing what you get and how happy you are 
with it. 

John Purser

If you pay for it, I think Dell is about the best out there.  I didn't
pay for anything extra and had my keyboard flake out on me about six
months after purchase.  I contacted Dell and FedEx dropped off a box the
next morning.  I convinced the FedEx driver to come back in 20 minutes
and off the laptop went.  Three days later the laptop was back here in
fine working order.

That's with the cheapest warranty they had.  If you pay for the nicer
warranties, the service you get is significantly better - and I
considered my service quite nice.

The only thing I haven't gotten working on my (2.5 year old) laptop is
advanced ACPI support for things like suspending it to memory and such.
 Their new laptops tend to have decent Linux support in every area
except for Wifi - and that may have changed recently.  It used to be to
use the built-in wifi cards, you had to use that ndiswrapper driver.

However, you said wifi didn't interest you.  :-)

Dell has reached out a bit to the Linux community.  They had somebody in
contact with the linux-dell-laptops mailing list a few years back and
set up a website and such with information on it, including laptop
information.  I don't know that they've invested *money* in Linux, but
they have spent at least a little bit of time to appease the community.

> to do precision work in jittery environments).  If there are other
> stable pointing devices that work adequately, I would like to hear

I've never heard of "IBM-style trackpoints" - Dell used to ship with
dual touchpad/pointingstick setups, but it seems they've dropped the
pointing sticks in favor of touchpads within the last two years.  Too
bad, I really liked the pointing sticks.  My touchpad does just fine for
me, though, and is quite adjustable in Linux.  If I drop the speed, etc,
down, it works fine in "jittery" environments.  What is the trackpoint
like, exactly?

> I want compute speed (2GHz), don't care too much about graphics
> speed, don't care at all about audio, games, etc.

A 1.5GHz Pentium M is likely going to outperform a 2GHz Pentium 4, FWIW.
 Don't get sucked in to the MHz Myth!  If your CPU speed isn't for
graphics or audio, what *is* it for?  Are you sure you need the CPU
power you think you need?

From athlonrob at axpr.net  Sat Oct  8 07:44:39 2005
From: athlonrob at axpr.net (AthlonRob)
Date: Sat, 08 Oct 2005 07:44:39 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Looking for new laptop vendor
In-Reply-To: <4347A438.2090408@gmail.com>
References: <20051008064531.GA6122@gate.kl-ic.com> <4347A438.2090408@gmail.com>
Message-ID: <4347DB57.5090600@axpr.net>

John Purser wrote:
> A couple of pieces of advice I received on components: Fast laptop
> drives are expensive and you're not going to notice the difference for
> most applications.

That's such a bad call.  :-)

Hard drives are the biggest bottleneck in computers today.  Applications
load faster, the system boots faster, if you swap out it becomes more
responsive... all with a faster hard drive.  And believe me, going from
a 4200RPM IBM drive to a 5400RPM Seagate drive in my laptop has given
this little system new life.  It went from sluggish to snappy.

Spend the money for the fast hard drive, you won't regret it.  They
aren't generally very expensive, either.  This 60GB 5400RPM drive with
8MB of cache (the 16MB cache Toshibas are also nice, but not as fast for
some reason) was somewhere around $80 from Newegg.

RAM and hard drive are the two most important components in a laptop for
performance.  A sluggish hard drive will kill the whole system... and
even the "fast" hard drives aren't as fast as their full-sized desktop
counterparts.  So get yourself a lot of RAM to compensate.  I wouldn't
even CONSIDER having less than 512MB, and wouldn't purchase a new laptop
today for myself with less than a gigabyte of RAM in it.

You'd find better general computing performance out of a laptop with the
slowest MHz rating available, a gig of RAM, and the fastest hard drive
available than you'd find with that same laptop with the fastest CPU,
half a gig of RAM, and a medium-grade hard drive.

Rob

	<4347A438.2090408@gmail.com> <4347DB57.5090600@axpr.net>

AthlonRob wrote:

>John Purser wrote:
>  
>
>>A couple of pieces of advice I received on components: Fast laptop
>>drives are expensive and you're not going to notice the difference for
>>most applications.
>>    
>>
>
>That's such a bad call.  :-)
>
>Hard drives are the biggest bottleneck in computers today.  Applications
>load faster, the system boots faster, if you swap out it becomes more
>responsive... all with a faster hard drive.  And believe me, going from
>a 4200RPM IBM drive to a 5400RPM Seagate drive in my laptop has given
>this little system new life.  It went from sluggish to snappy.
>
>Spend the money for the fast hard drive, you won't regret it.  They
>aren't generally very expensive, either.  This 60GB 5400RPM drive with
>8MB of cache (the 16MB cache Toshibas are also nice, but not as fast for
>some reason) was somewhere around $80 from Newegg.
>
>RAM and hard drive are the two most important components in a laptop for
>performance.  A sluggish hard drive will kill the whole system... and
>even the "fast" hard drives aren't as fast as their full-sized desktop
>counterparts.  So get yourself a lot of RAM to compensate.  I wouldn't
>even CONSIDER having less than 512MB, and wouldn't purchase a new laptop
>today for myself with less than a gigabyte of RAM in it.
>
>You'd find better general computing performance out of a laptop with the
>slowest MHz rating available, a gig of RAM, and the fastest hard drive
>available than you'd find with that same laptop with the fastest CPU,
>half a gig of RAM, and a medium-grade hard drive.
>
>Rob
>_______________________________________________
>PLUG mailing list
>PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
>http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug
>
>  
>

On 8 Oct 2005, at 7:38, AthlonRob wrote:

> > 3)  I strongly prefer the IBM-style trackpoint to touchpads (Easier
> > to do precision work in jittery environments).  If there are other
> > stable pointing devices that work adequately, I would like to hear
> > about those, too.
> 
> I've never heard of "IBM-style trackpoints" - Dell used to ship with
> dual touchpad/pointingstick setups, but it seems they've dropped the
> pointing sticks in favor of touchpads within the last two years.  Too
> bad, I really liked the pointing sticks.  My touchpad does just fine
> for me, though, and is quite adjustable in Linux.  If I drop the
> speed, etc, down, it works fine in "jittery" environments.  What is
> the trackpoint like, exactly?

My Compaq came with a feature that I dearly love - an on-off switch 
for the touchpad. (All Compaqs have this, I think.) It's so annoying 
to have your thumb accidentally hit the touchpad while typing.

I had a laptop before with the trackpoint thing and I hated it. But I 
also dislike touchpads. I went to Fry's and gave them about $30 for 
a Logitech optical cordless USB mouse. The USB receiver is a 
litltle stick that looks like a flash drive, so there are no wires. It's 
only a couple inches long. It even comes in various colors, so I got 
one in silver to match the laptop. It has a little fabric case that 
holds the mouse and USB stick.

When I boot the laptop the touchpad is automatically working. If I 
forget to put the USB receiver in a USB port before turning the 
computer on, I can stick it in anytime and the mouse then works 
automatically too. The USB hotplug system works just like it is 
supposed to.

I thought of just disabling the touchpad since I don't like it, but it's 
nice to have in case I get somewhere and forgot the mouse at 
home, or the batteries died, etc. -- although the two AA batteries 
last a long time, and the mouse has an on-off switch on the 
bottom, too. The receiver, mouse with batteries and case weigh 
only 150 grams. Getting this little mouse was one of my better 
moves.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sat Oct  8 08:38:32 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sat, 8 Oct 2005 08:38:32 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Two screen presentation software
In-Reply-To: <20051008071037.GA6460@gate.kl-ic.com>
References: <20051008071037.GA6460@gate.kl-ic.com>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.62.0510080820280.6073@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, 8 Oct 2005, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> One of the things I observed was the relentless sequential nature of a
> typical Powerpoint slide show performance; Jeff and his friend had their
> pitch rehearsed, and found it very difficult to change order in order to
> answer the VC's questions. I think some of that is the paradigm imposed on
> them by their software, which does not easily support skipping around.

Keith,

   I cannot comment on the dual nature of viewing different screens locally
and via the external projector; my notebooks display the same thing as they
push out the external monitor port. That written, I can offer what may be the
solution you seek: The "beamer" class in LaTeX/LyX. I use this for my
presentations (two in the past month) and it's the best computer-generated
slide preparation/display system I've used over the past dozen or so years.

   Ignoring the ease of preparation and control, as well as the typeset output
(particularly with math), I'll explain the ability to navigate among the
slides.

   The slides are compiled to PDF so any Adobe Reader can display them. I
create the presentations on my linux system and show them on the local
Microsoft system; transfer is via USB 'thumb' drive. This format also permits
me to send the slides as an e-mail attachment that anyone can view.

   Along the bottom of each slide are a series of very faint images. These are
used to navigate around the presentation. From the left to right they let the
presenter move back and forward by slide, frame, subsection, section, and
beginning/end. Putting acroread into full-screen display mode (ctrl-l), and
the cursor on the advance-one-slide icon, allows me to advance the display by
pressing the left mouse button. The cursor is invisible, but still positioned
in the proper place.

   If I had to navigate to a slide whose location I did not remember, I would
press ctrl-l to reduce the display size and expose the presentation layout tree
in Reader's separate frame on the left. Here is display the presentation
outline by section and subsection. Clicking on the appropriate heading
displays the appropriate slide. Full screen display can then be restored.

   It's a fabulous system, better than foil, prosper, seminar, and other LaTeX
slide classes. I'll send a copy of the presentation I gave to the BLM last
week to anyone who requests it (761K file) so you can see how easy it is to
navigate. Another great feature is that the outline is shown at the top of
each frame so the audience knows where you are in the context of the whole
presentation.

   As I wrote, it works great in LyX, too, which makes creating an application
very fast and convenient -- except for the thinking, of course. I've become a
very big fan of LaTeX/LyX. I use the Beamer class for slide preparation and I
use PSTricks to create vector graphics directly in PostScript; they can be
included in a LaTeX document when compiled with ps4pdf. What a great set of
tools for technical writing!

HTH,

Rich

From aaron at bavariati.org  Sat Oct  8 09:27:35 2005

Subject: [PLUG] Asterisk hints?
In-Reply-To: <20051008053817.GB9439@merlot.com>
References: <20051008053817.GB9439@merlot.com>
Message-ID: <20051008162735.GG16603@bavariati.org>

On Fri, Oct 07, 2005 at 10:38:17PM -0700, Kurt Sussman wrote:
> I found out that my nice little PBX appliance, a 3 year old Talkswitch
> 24, can't be adjusted to work with the impedance of my Packet8 ATA, so I
> get a nasty echo when it forwards calls to remote extensions. 

Sounds dubious to me.  What happens when you plug a phone directly
into the ATA?  Have you tried a different ATA?

> For the $700 that a new Talkswitch will cost, I should be able to
> build a perfectly adequate (or better) Asterisk box, right?

The phone-line interface bits aren't cheap.  Digium FxO/FxS cards
start in the $300 range.  The cards support up to 4 lines, so it
sounds like you could do with a 1-FxO (for the landline) and 3-FxS
(for the phone stations) setup on one card.

Another way is to use an ATA for the stations.  Atcom makes a decent
4-FxS ATA for pretty cheap.  Then use one of the $100 softmodem-based
1-FxO cards for the landline.

> I currently have one analog line and one VoIP line. If I can get a good
> deal on raw VoIP lines I could use two of those, but I'm keeping one
> analog line for emergencies. 

Good idea.  There are wholesale VoIP line vendors that support
Asterisk's IAX protocol, which is more robust than SIP.

From plug at merlot.com  Sat Oct  8 10:36:00 2005

Aaron Burt (aaron at bavariati.org) typed this ...
> On Fri, Oct 07, 2005 at 10:38:17PM -0700, Kurt Sussman wrote:
> > I found out that my nice little PBX appliance, a 3 year old Talkswitch
> > 24, can't be adjusted to work with the impedance of my Packet8 ATA, so I
> > get a nasty echo when it forwards calls to remote extensions. 
> 
> Sounds dubious to me.  What happens when you plug a phone directly
> into the ATA?  Have you tried a different ATA?

The problem is that the call comes in on a Qwest line at impedance A,
then goes out to the remote extension (through the ATA) at impedance B.
That's where the echo comes from. By itself, the ATA works fine.

> > For the $700 that a new Talkswitch will cost, I should be able to
> > build a perfectly adequate (or better) Asterisk box, right?
> 
> The phone-line interface bits aren't cheap.  Digium FxO/FxS cards
> start in the $300 range.  The cards support up to 4 lines, so it
> sounds like you could do with a 1-FxO (for the landline) and 3-FxS
> (for the phone stations) setup on one card.

Yeah, I've seen those on ebay. Not cheap.

> Another way is to use an ATA for the stations.  Atcom makes a decent
> 4-FxS ATA for pretty cheap.  Then use one of the $100 softmodem-based
> 1-FxO cards for the landline.

Why not a Digium FXO clone for $10 (on ebay)?

> > I currently have one analog line and one VoIP line. If I can get a good
> > deal on raw VoIP lines I could use two of those, but I'm keeping one
> > analog line for emergencies. 
> 
> Good idea.  There are wholesale VoIP line vendors that support
> Asterisk's IAX protocol, which is more robust than SIP.

Looks like most of them are around $2/month + $.03/minute. That's pretty
good.

Thanks.

--Kurt

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Sat Oct  8 11:24:55 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)

Subject: [PLUG] Two screen presentation software
In-Reply-To: <20051008071037.GA6460@gate.kl-ic.com>

Message-ID: <c29537840510081124y23005302nd09fa58998be68fe@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/8/05, Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com> wrote:
>
> Thursday morning I attended an AEA event, a "mock VC" presentation.
> My friend Jeff Zurshmeide and a partner did a mock presentation of
> their imaginary startup to a committee of three local venture
> capitalists, followed by their evaluation meeting.  We got to see
> how they perform the process;  very educational.  However, that is
> not what I am writing about here.
>
> One of the things I observed was the relentless sequential nature of a
> typical Powerpoint slide show performance;  Jeff and his friend had
> their pitch rehearsed, and found it very difficult to change order
> in order to answer the VC's questions.  I think some of that is the
> paradigm imposed on them by their software, which does not easily
> support skipping around.
>
> Most modern laptops have two complete video generation systems;  it
> is possible to display one image on the laptop screen,  and send an
> entirely different image out the VGA/DVI port to the projector.  A
> next-generation presentation program could make good use of this
> capability.
>
> This means the presenter can be looking at a "control panel", with
> perhaps a quarter of the laptop screen devoted to the projected
> image, and the other three quarters for script, messaging, notes,
> slide sorter, etc.  Further, for situations where audience input
> redirects the order of the slides, the software should adapt to that,
> allowing the presenter to resequence in real time, not touching on
> the out-of-order slide again, perhaps adding backup slides or removing
> slides from the presentation in real time.   Slides could be made with
> "hotspots", indicated areas on the user's panel that cause some
> animation (like text lining up, or movement in part of a diagram)
> to happen on the projected screen.  Imagine a flowchart or data flow
> diagram, where you can make "data" appear to move down the path you
> are talking about by moving your mouse over it on your control panel.
>
> I don't know of any presentation software that offers these capabilities,
> but it might not be difficult to do under Linux.  Is there anything
> like this out there?  If not, is anyone inspired to do this as an
> open source project?

I hate to say it, but Powerpoint already offers this capability for
dual-head Windows boxes.  The gist of it is the slideshow is displayed
on the secondary monitor (i.e. the output on the laptop) while the
main powerpoint window remains displayed on the primary monitor,
including the sidebar with slide previews that allows you to quickly
jump around.  You can even enter notes for your presentation that are
displayed on the laptop screen but not on the actual slideshow.

I do not know if such capabilities are possible with other software,
but given how much trouble I've had with configuring dual-head on
demand, I think there are some other issues to overcome first.  For
example, it is not possible (that I know of) to add a second display
to your system without restarting X, thus forcing you to restart all X
applications you have running.  In the past I've overcome this by
using the text login and two different xorg.conf files, but there
really should be a way to change this on the fly, so you don't have to
kill X.

Of course, I'm just ranting without providing a patch, so I guess I
don't really have any right to complain :-)

this for dual-head on demand.

Message-ID: <200510082033.j98KXOL2090197@m5p.com>

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> 1)  Demonstrated excellent repair service.  Laptops are subject to
> a lot of stress, and *will fail* - I want my next laptop to come 
> from a company with a demonstrated history of quick repairs with no
> nonsense.  As I said, that used to be IBM.  Who does it now?  Has
> anyone here got any positive factory service stories to share?

I'll second the mention of Toshiba making pretty reliable laptops. The
reviews I've heard are fairly old as the folks with those laptops still
have them.

Panasonic is another one to consider. I've got no idea about their
service, but their "ToughBooks" are designed to take major abuse and so
aren't supposed to need it. I've heard they do tend to last. I vaguely
recall a mention that the other Panasonic laptops tend to be fairly
rugged, but no certainty here.

> 2)  Mostly Linux compatable.  I want the display and the ethernet
> and the mouse-surrogate to work.  I don't need working built-in
> wifi.  For software compatability reasons, I need Intel architecture.

Alas that rules out /current/ Apple laptops. Apple has a good reputation,
but of course they're PowerPC. They might make an amd64 laptop in the
future, though my current information is PPC is still far ahead in power
consumption (delaying the transition for laptops).

> I want compute speed (2GHz), don't care too much about graphics
> speed, don't care at all about audio, games, etc.  While high 
> reliability would be nice, it is impossible to evaluate, as the
> machines with reliability track records are all obsolete, and the
> various brandname sources all use the same manufacturers anyway.

What is the scale for the 2GHz number? Athlon 64 2GHz? Pentium M 2GHz?
Pentium 4 2GHz?

The Pentium Ms (present on "Centrino" laptops, or those that explicitly
say "Pentium M" rather than "Pentium 4M") appear to be derived from the
Pentium III core, and provide *far* superior performance per clock cycle
in addition to better power consumption. The Athlon 64s match fairly well
to Pentium Ms of the same clock speed, and both masacre Pentium 4(M)s of
the same clock speed. I'm unsure of the exact matchup, but a P-M 1.6GHz
should compare favorably to a P4 2.4GHz.

# from Jason R. Martin
# on Saturday 08 October 2005 01:01 pm:

>Hmm, this will require some experimentation. ?My laptop has an Nvidia
>graphics chip in it, and I'm not sure how the second display is
>exported.

On my (desktop) ge-force 4 card, the X config looks like so:

#########
Section "Device"
    Identifier  "NVIDIA GeForce4 0"
    Driver      "nvidia"
    VendorName  "TI4600"
    BusID       "PCI:1:5:0"
        Option        "UseInt10Module" "on"
    Screen      0
EndSection

Section "Device"
    Identifier  "NVIDIA GeForce4 1"
    Driver      "nvidia"
    VendorName  "TI4600"
    BusID       "PCI:1:5:0"
    Screen      1
EndSection
#########

According to XF86Config-4, the "Screen" value is mandatory for cards 
that can drive more than one display.  The BusID entries are because 
I'm using a second card for the third head.

IIRC, the matrox cards are setup slightly differently, but it's been too 
long since I've messed with those.

>From: "Jason R. Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> On 10/8/05, Eric Wilhelm <scratchcomputing at gmail.com> wrote:
> > According to XF86Config-4, the "Screen" value is mandatory for cards
> > that can drive more than one display.  The BusID entries are because
> > I'm using a second card for the third head.
> 
> Yes, but the question was whether the open-source nv driver supports a
> setup like this.  I dislike using the close-source driver, and in fact

It does. It isn't so much a proprietary extension, as a proprietary
method of configuring it. From the outside it looks and behaves just like
Xinerama, except with better performance because the graphics engine
handles both displays at the same time rather than having to replicate
the drawing commands for two displays.

> would avoid Nvidia (and ATI) altogether if I could, but the Intel
> graphics that come with Centrino laptops are pitiful (and usually
> lower resolution).  If I can ever get out of Windows today I'll
> experiment.

Unfortunately for you, you've named all three of the remaining
non-embedded graphics chip companies. The former two are also aiming for
the embedded market (though a much more powerful one than the traditional
embedded market).

At least good drivers exist for the former two. The closed-source nVIDIA
driver works extremely well. The open-source ATi driver does 2D just
fine.

> > IIRC, the matrox cards are setup slightly differently, but it's been too
> > long since I've messed with those.
> 
> Ah, wish I could go back to Matrox.  Anybody know if they make a
> mobile version of the Millenium cards?  I'd buy that for a dollar...

To my knowledge no one has bought Matrox yet, but I wouldn't be surprised
if it happens at some point here (or failing that they go bankrupt). Good
luck finding a laptop with a Matrox chip, the above three own the entire
market now. Unless someone has a stockpile of them they're selling off
you're unlikely to find a new Matrox chip in the next couple years.

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Sat Oct  8 17:27:40 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 08 Oct 2005 17:27:40 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] kdialog/kmonologue??
Message-ID: <86slvbedqb.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


I am writing a set of scripts on Knoppix (derived from the recent
4.0.2 version) to facilitate some unskilled-in-Linux/
unskilled-in-computers folks doing some data collection for us.  I am
not a KDE person in my normal life, but while researching how to
facilitate these operations on Knoppix, I found kdialog, which does
some graphic user interaction and is like to be handy for dressing
things up a bit.

One of the things I'd like to do, but that I don't see that kdialog
supports, is a kind of scrolling output monitoring window (i.e., the
stdout of some process) with a "Quit" button.  Right now, in
developer==user mode, I quit with Ctrl-C in an xterm.  I don't have
the time to write anything elaborate to do this myself.  

I am thinking of an interface either like this:

  $ foo | bar

where foo is a process (that I supply) that outputs information and
bar (which is what I am looking for) displays it, and kills foo when
bar quits (or provides enough information to do it in a shell script
using pid's).  Or maybe an interface like:

  $ bar --command=foo

Any ideas?

I did not say that the closed-source nvidia driver didn't work well, I
have used it.  I object to it because it requires a closed source
binary kernel module, and my personal view is that it violates the GPL
requirements of the kernel.  Others disagree with this, and perhaps we
will never reach a consensus on it, but the fact remains that I would
prefer (if possible) to support a company who provides open-source
drivers for their products.

I think Matrox still has a foothold in the workstation market, since
they pretty much specialize in dual/triple-head displays with strong
2D graphics for photo/video editing.  I guess they have PCIe Parhelia
stuff now, which I didn't know about until today.  I assumed they
hadn't added anything that wasn't AGP.

Intel as opposed to Athlon? Or Intel as opposed to PowerPC/Macintosh? I

while. Toshibas used to have them, but they've gone the touchpad route 
lately as well. With the add-on Synaptics code, I've gotten so used to 
the touchpad that I might find it difficult to go back to a trackpoint. 
For example, a one-finger tap is a left click, a two-finger tap is a 
middle click and a three-finger tap is a right click.

machine that can support the largest RAM and, as someone suggested, pay 
the extra $US for a faster hard drive. How critical is lightness and 
power efficiency to you? I think you said you need rugged, so if you can 
handle some weight and don't mind running on a power cord when you need 
the speed, you can probably afford to put in lots of RAM and a fast hard 
drive.

"Show me the money". I'll believe it is viable when they've actually got
hardware to sell. They'll also need an EvilOS driver as well (dual-boot
machines).

> > At least good drivers exist for the former two. The closed-source nVIDIA
> > driver works extremely well. The open-source ATi driver does 2D just

> 
> I did not say that the closed-source nvidia driver didn't work well, I
> have used it.  I object to it because it requires a closed source
> binary kernel module, and my personal view is that it violates the GPL
> requirements of the kernel.  Others disagree with this, and perhaps we
> will never reach a consensus on it, but the fact remains that I would
> prefer (if possible) to support a company who provides open-source
> drivers for their products.

Both have open source drivers, but those don't do anywhere near the
amount of 3D acceleration that the closed source ones do. It is quite
possible for software to do the remaining pieces, but you still lose
performance.

The generally cited reason for them being closed source is to conceal
patent violations that aren't known about. They aren't knowingly
violating patents, but there are too many landmines to risk it. The Open
Graphics folks will have to deal with this when it arises, will they
survive the experience?

> > > Ah, wish I could go back to Matrox.  Anybody know if they make a

Where did you see mention of a PCIe Parhelia? The Parhelia looked to be
a failure, slower and much more expensive than anything out of ATi or
nVIDIA. If they really do still have a foothold in the workstation
market, I'd hazard a guess that it is mostly folks who say "no one was
ever fired for buying Matrox". There aren't any Matrox chips I consider
viable on their own merits unless three displays really is a crucial
feature to you.


>From: "M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net>
> Keith Lofstrom wrote:
> >Most modern laptops have two complete video generation systems;  it
> >is possible to display one image on the laptop screen,  and send an
> >entirely different image out the VGA/DVI port to the projector.  A
> >next-generation presentation program could make good use of this
> >capability.
> >
> I was not aware of that ... I'll take a look at my Compaq Presario.

Neither ATi nor nVIDIA make any chips that can handle less than two heads
at this point. There are some laptops and cards where this ability is
disabled, but they *all* support two monitors. Apple did manage to
thoroughly disable it on their iBooks with nVIDIA chips, but nVIDIA never
made a laptop chip without the ability. By contrast a simple OpenFirmware
program can reenable it on iBooks with ATi chips.

From lowbassman at gmail.com  Mon Oct 10 19:29:10 2005
From: lowbassman at gmail.com (Matt Alexander)
Date: Mon, 10 Oct 2005 19:29:10 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID for remote filesystems
Message-ID: <9e0a35780510101929y41e1dca4sa909e715247f23de@mail.gmail.com>

I've basically accomplished what I wanted by using the loop device. Another
option that I played with briefly, but should also work, is by using the
Network Block Device.

Here's a brief tutorial:

I have 4 remote filesystems mounted. One is using sshfs, one is using Samba
from a Windows server, and 2 are using NFS from two different NetApp filers.

I then create a 1G file on each mount point and also one on my local file
system...

dd if=/dev/zero of=/sshfs/matt0 bs=1M count=1024
dd if=/dev/zero of=/samba/matt1 bs=1M count=1024
dd if=/dev/zero of=/nfs1/matt2 bs=1M count=1024
dd if=/dev/zero of=/nfs2/matt3 bs=1M count=1024
dd if=/dev/zero of=/home/matt/matt4 bs=1M count=1024

I then create a loop device for each file...

losetup /dev/loop0 /sshfs/matt0
losetup /dev/loop1 /samba/matt1
losetup /dev/loop2 /nfs1/matt2
losetup /dev/loop3 /nfs2/matt3
losetup /dev/loop4 /home/matt/matt4

I then create a RAID5 array from the loop devices...

mdadm --create /dev/md0 --level=5 --raid-devices=5 /dev/loop0 /dev/loop1
/dev/loop2 /dev/loop3 /dev/loop4

I then create a filesystem and mount it...

mkfs.ext3 /dev/md0
mount /dev/md0 /mnt/matt

Message-ID: <1129090306.7635.9.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

I'm not going to answer your questions in specifics but in general

dpkg -S `which knetattach` will show you what the source for a package
is if it's currently installed in your system.

apt-cache search knetattach will tell you if apt, knows about your
package
apt-cache show knetattach will tell you more detailed information about
the package.

Make sure you have network addresses included in
your /etc/apt/sources.list file to get new/update packages.  Installing
from a CD is only the first step.

The other thing you can do is go to http://package.debian.org and search
for your program.


Sean

you can get to it from the Configure button on the Network Moniter
applet.

of the Networks it can "hear" when you run the program. You can also
chose to use dhcp or set a static ip address there.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Wed Oct 12 10:35:36 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Wed, 12 Oct 2005 10:35:36 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Working with PDF's
In-Reply-To: <20051012161504.34051.qmail@web50609.mail.yahoo.com>
References: <434C92C6.8070906@cesmail.net>
	<20051012161504.34051.qmail@web50609.mail.yahoo.com>
Message-ID: <20051012173536.GN16603@bavariati.org>

On Wed, Oct 12, 2005 at 09:15:03AM -0700, Robert Kopp wrote:
> --- "M. Edward (Ed) Borasky" <znmeb at cesmail.net>  wrote:
> 
> > How about Scribus?
> >
> I've heard about that. What I need to do is convert
> .doc to .pdf and then arrange the pages to look like a
> booklet, so the printer can use it in that form. 

Ah, thank you for describing what you want to do.

OpenOffice Writer makes nice PDFs from word-processing documents,
including Wicrosoft Merde .doc files.

But you'll actually have the easiest time if you can get your Webster
to print to a PostScript file.  That way, any formatting or layout
issues are entirely in that person's hands, rather than yours.

Typically, for a booklet layout, I use PSTools.  "psbook" rearranges a
Postscript file's page order to make booklets of various sizes.
By default, it assumes you're folding the whole stack of pages in
half, which at higher page-counts becomes impractical.  In those
cases, you can set the signature to a foldable number of pages,
such as 16.  The printer should be able to suggest a number.

I then use "psnup -2 -pletter" to rescale and arrange the pages 2-up
on letter-size paper.  The "impose" utility is also nice for this,
since it senses the actual document size and scales accordingly.

The printer will likely want PDF, so you can use ps2pdf or something to
convert the Postscript-format booklet into PDF.

This is all commandline stuff, which is nice since you can script it,
do it consistently and even automatically.

But who reads long emails anyway,
  Aaron

Yes, I figured out the problem for MY machine. It was a marginal 
CD-Drive. I replaced it with another drive and was able to continue with 
the installation.

Here's part of the last message I sent the list on the subject:

------------------------------------------------------------------------
MARGINAL CD DRIVE WAS THE PROBLEM.

I changed the CD drive for another I had in an otherwise dead machine, 
and tried again, this time it boots and runs the install just fine.

Since I had installed SuSE 9.1 on the machine, and upgrading from the CD 
set to 9.3 is an option, I'm giving that a try to see how it goes. If it 
is clean, I may use that approach to upgrade this system -- but only 
after backups, of course.

There's only 5:36:21 to go, so I'll have to wait to declare success. 
Hmmm. Now there's 5:42:28, and it says that it's looking at CD2 even 
though CD 2 is sitting on my desk!

Be sure to tune in next week for the continuation of our saga.
------------------------------------------------------------------------

I'm afraid I never sent anything regarding how it finally turned out. In 
spite of the "time remaining" and other install messages, it finally did 
finish and is a working installation. I haven't had time to use it, or 
to upgrade this machine since then, but I'm satisfied that the process 
will work when I get to it.

From heinlein at madboa.com  Wed Oct 12 11:32:33 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Wed, 12 Oct 2005 11:32:33 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] time based grep
In-Reply-To: <20051012175135.GA23308@subtend.net>
References: <20051012175135.GA23308@subtend.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0510121121500.8477@arrowhead.galois.com>

On Wed, 12 Oct 2005, Kris wrote:

> My goal is to write a nagios plugin which grep's the last
> 30 minutes of /var/log/dhcp.log for "no free leases".

sed -ne "/^$(date -d '30 min ago' +'%b %e %H:%M:')/,/\$/p" dhcp.log |\
   grep 'no free leases'

This assumes that dhcp.log will contain at least one timestamped entry 
per minute. Why that's so is an exercise left to the reader. :-)

-- 
Paul "pipes are my friend" Heinlein

From wcooley at nakedape.cc  Wed Oct 12 18:29:54 2005
From: wcooley at nakedape.cc (Wil Cooley)
Date: Wed, 12 Oct 2005 18:29:54 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] time based grep
In-Reply-To: <20051012175135.GA23308@subtend.net>
References: <20051012175135.GA23308@subtend.net>
Message-ID: <1129166994.19480.35.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>

On Wed, 2005-10-12 at 10:51 -0700, Kris wrote:
> My goal is to write a nagios plugin which grep's the last 
> 30 minutes of /var/log/dhcp.log for "no free leases".
> 
> Currently I'm seeing no easy way to do this without much script-fu.  Anyone 
> have a trick for this?  My other option is to set log rotation to 30 
> minutes and have a lot of logfiles.

While not exactly what you're looking for, there are a few utilities
which will maintain a record of where they last looked at a log file and
return only the entries since.  I'm thinking specifically of 'logtail'
from Psionic's 'logcheck' project; 'epylog' also has a similar
capability.  Might be hard to find since Cisco subsumed Psionic, taking
logcheck and portsentry down with it; the source is on my FTP server:
http://ftp.nakedape.cc/nakedape/misc/logcheck/

Wil

Wil Cooley <wcooley at nakedape.cc>
Naked Ape Consulting, Ltd. <http://nakedape.cc>

From krisa at subtend.net  Thu Oct 13 01:29:50 2005
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Thu, 13 Oct 2005 01:29:50 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] time based grep
In-Reply-To: <1129166994.19480.35.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
References: <20051012175135.GA23308@subtend.net>
	<1129166994.19480.35.camel@spartacus.nakedape.priv>
Message-ID: <20051013082950.GA7334@subtend.net>

On Wed, Oct 12, 2005 at 06:29:54PM -0700, Wil Cooley wrote:
> On Wed, 2005-10-12 at 10:51 -0700, Kris wrote:
> > My goal is to write a nagios plugin which grep's the last 
> > 30 minutes of /var/log/dhcp.log for "no free leases".
> > 
> > Currently I'm seeing no easy way to do this without much script-fu.  Anyone 
> > have a trick for this?  My other option is to set log rotation to 30 
> > minutes and have a lot of logfiles.
> 
> While not exactly what you're looking for, there are a few utilities
> which will maintain a record of where they last looked at a log file and
> return only the entries since.  I'm thinking specifically of 'logtail'
> from Psionic's 'logcheck' project; 'epylog' also has a similar
> capability.  Might be hard to find since Cisco subsumed Psionic, taking
> logcheck and portsentry down with it; the source is on my FTP server:
> http://ftp.nakedape.cc/nakedape/misc/logcheck/

Turns out I didn't look closely enough at the check_log nagios plugin,
which does exactly what I want to:

"On the first run of the plugin, it will return an OK state with a message 
of "Log check data initialized".  On successive runs, it will return an OK 
state if *no* pattern matches have been found in the *difference* between 
the log file and the older copy of the log file.  If the plugin detects 
any pattern matches in the log diff, it will return a CRITICAL state and 
print out a message is the following format: "(x) last_match", where "x" 
is the total number of pattern matches found in the file and "last_match" 
is the last entry in the log file which matches the pattern."

Sweet.  This is even useful outside of Nagios.

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

From aaron at bavariati.org  Thu Oct 13 10:18:18 2005
From: aaron at bavariati.org (Aaron Burt)
Date: Thu, 13 Oct 2005 10:18:18 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Ubuntu on Virtual PC
In-Reply-To: <868FE291-DF90-44FE-9EB6-EA18A3C87AF7@braithwaites.net>

On Thu, Oct 13, 2005 at 09:54:48AM -0700, Ron Braithwaite wrote:
> I'm trying to get Ubuntu running on a Mac using Virtual PC and am  
> having problems getting the video right.
> 
> Anyone else here got it going successfully and what did it take?

Google sez you gotta edit xorg.conf (or XF86Config-4 on older
versions) and change DefaultDepth from 24 to 16.

More here:
http://wiki.ubuntu.com//HowToConfigureUbuntuForMicrosoftVirtualPC2004

Google knows all,
  Aaron

Subject: [PLUG] ATI 9250 Sapphire Drivers
Message-ID: <434F2927.8030203@verizon.net>

I'm a new Linux user. I'd been meaning to get started using Linux on my 
own computer for a long time. However, as I am now taking a class on 
Linux at CCC, I now have an excuse. My initial intent was to get a 
second hard drive, add that to my existing computer, and load Linux on 
that. However, I have come into one problem - my graphics card is an ATI 
9250 Sapphire. I am aware that ATI graphics cards have had pretty poor 
Linux Driver support, so I wasn't suprised that I hadn't found any. 
However, as I'd rather not build another computer, or buy another 
graphics card, I'm hoping to a certain extent that the only reason I 
hadn't found any Linux drivers for the ATI 9250 was because I just 
wasn't looking hard enough. Is this the case? And if there aren't any 
drivers for my graphics card, what's a good graphics card with good 
Linux support that I could pick up to replace it?

The Linux distro I'm planning on installing is Fedora Core 3 (if that 
helps at all). If you need any other system specifications, let me know.
