From alan at clueserver.org  Thu Oct 13 21:49:38 2005
From: alan at clueserver.org (Alan)
Date: Thu, 13 Oct 2005 21:49:38 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] ANNOUNCEMENT: Advanced Topics Oct 19th, 2005
Message-ID: <1129265378.15781.4.camel@dagon>

Portland Linux/Unix Group Advanced Topics

Speaker:            Rob Wehrli

Subject:            Qt Designer HOWTO
                 
Date:               Oct 19th 2005
Time:               7:00pm - 9:00pm

Location:           Jax 826 SW 2nd Ave
                    Portland, OR

Join us for a Qt Designer HOWTO presentation by Rob Wehrli!

Rob Wehrli will demonstrate using Qt Designer on GNU/Linux to produce
X  
Window graphical user interface applications written in C++.  Rob will  
discuss some of the nuances and annoyances with using Qt Designer
(v3.3)  
while showing how to build an application from start to finish.  Note
that  
this will not be a Qt API-related discussion!  No prior Qt application  
development exposure is required.  Basic C/C++ skills are helpful,
though  
not necessarily required.  Slated for discussion are:


1: Qt Designer General Overview
-what is it?
-how do I get it?
-what can I do with it?
-what can't it do?

2: Using Qt Designer - Basics
-projects & project settings
-basic filesystem structure

3: Hello, World Qt Designer Style
-create a new C++ project
-add a Dialog to the project
-add some widgets to the Dialog
-previewing the GUI
-compile and run (with various styles)

4: Customizing Qt Designer
-fonts and syntax color coding
-templates

5: Questions and Answers (maybe, dumb looks are still free...)

Standard meeting rules apply.

From carla at bratgrrl.com  Fri Oct 14 08:28:37 2005
From: carla at bratgrrl.com (Carla Schroder)
Date: Fri, 14 Oct 2005 08:28:37 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] wvdial, pppd, and permissions insanity
Message-ID: <200510140828.37223.carla@bratgrrl.com>

OK gang, this is driving me nuts.

I want to let non-privileged users run wvdial. It works on Debian. Here is how 
it's set up:

$ ls -l `which wvdial`
-rwxr-xr-x ?1 root root 98408 Jan 21 ?2005 /usr/bin/wvdial

$ ls -l `which wvdialconf`
-rwxr-xr-x ?1 root root 48968 Jan 21 ?2005 /usr/bin/wvdialconf

$ ls -l /usr/sbin/pppd
-rwsr-xr-- ?1 root dip 232536 Dec 30 ?2004 /usr/sbin/pppd

$ ls -l /dev/ttyS0
crw-rw---- ?1 root dialout 4, 64 Oct 14 00:31 /dev/ttyS0

Easy peasey. I don't have to change a thing. This lets users set up their own 
private dialup configs in 
~/.wvdialrc, and also use the global config in /etc/wvdial.conf. No problems, 
every one is happy, the sun shines, life is good. Any user who need dialup 
services merely needs to be put in the 'dialout' group, with no other 
changes. 

Then we come to CentOS, which is a Red hat clone. (cue ominous music).

$ ls -l /dev/ttyS2
crw-rw---- ?1 root uucp 4, 64 Oct 14 00:31 /dev/ttyS2

$ ls -l /usr/sbin/pppd
-r-x-xr-x 1 root root 250996 Feb 21 ?2005 /usr/sbin/pppd

$ ls -l `which wvdial`
-rwxr-xr-x ?1 root root 127636 Feb 21 ?2005 /usr/bin/wvdial

$ ls -l `which wvdialconf`
-rwxr-xr-x ?1 root root 73912 Feb 21 ?2005 /usr/bin/wvdialconf

To get wvdial and wvdialconf to work for unprivileged user I have to ?chmod 
4755 (ooo suid, what a 
good idea :P.) Assigning groups doesn't matter- for example, changing the 
group ownsership of wvdial 
and wvdialconf to uucp, then adding human users to uucp still doesn't make 
them accessible. So with suid I can at least set up some private accounts in 
the user's homedir. But the users cannot access global accounts 
in /etc/wvdial.conf

Nothing I do gets ordinary unprivileged users out of their homedirs- not suid 
on pppd, nor stuffing everone into the same groups- nothing. I get the same 
error: 'Cannot open device foo. Device or resource busy.'

Yes, I'm sure I have the correct serial port- it works for the root user.

All brilliant notions welcome.

Please note I have googled a lot and found the same problem all over. One 
suggested solution was using an alias file containing USERCTL=yes. 
(http://www.redhat.com/docs/manuals/linux/RHL-9-Manual/ref-guide/s1-networkscripts-interfaces.html)
Tried that. Didn't work.

--

and sysadmin's guide! http://www.oreilly.com/catalog/linuxckbk/

From sean.whitney at gmail.com  Fri Oct 14 11:29:33 2005
From: sean.whitney at gmail.com (Sean Whitney)
Date: Fri, 14 Oct 2005 11:29:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] (no subject)
In-Reply-To: <101420051636.15090.434FDE9D00076DE400003AF22200734076CDC0CE0109019F@comcast.net>
References: <101420051636.15090.434FDE9D00076DE400003AF22200734076CDC0CE0109019F@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <1129314573.7635.17.camel@einstein.seansdomain.org>

On Fri, 2005-10-14 at 16:36 +0000, pogo162 at comcast.net wrote:
> I'm running Debian 3.1 and I can't get it to play DVD's.  I've used
> Totem and Ogle.  Totem comes back with an error saying 'failed to open
> device /dev/hdc for reading: Permission denied',

This sounds like you need to get the permissions straight on you dvd
drive.

On my debian box

ls -al /dev/hdc
brw-rw----  1 root cdrom 22, 0 2004-02-08 18:35 /dev/hdc

Which tells me that I have to be a member of the group cdrom to access
this device

grep cdrom /etc/group
cdrom:x:24:sean,hal

shows me that I (username sean) am a member of this group.  Make sure
your username is listed on this line, or whatever the group owner
for /dev/hdc is on your box.


Sean

-------------- Original message -------------- 

> On Fri, 2005-10-14 at 16:36 +0000, pogo162 at comcast.net wrote: 
> > I'm running Debian 3.1 and I can't get it to play DVD's. I've used 
> > Totem and Ogle. Totem comes back with an error saying 'failed to open 
> > device /dev/hdc for reading: Permission denied', 
> 
> This sounds like you need to get the permissions straight on you dvd 
> drive. 
> 
> On my debian box 
> 
> ls -al /dev/hdc 
> brw-rw---- 1 root cdrom 22, 0 2004-02-08 18:35 /dev/hdc 
> 
> Which tells me that I have to be a member of the group cdrom to access 
> this device 
> 
> grep cdrom /etc/group 
> cdrom:x:24:sean,hal 
> 
> shows me that I (username sean) am a member of this group. Make sure 
> your username is listed on this line, or whatever the group owner 
> for /dev/hdc is on your box. 
> 
> 
> Sean

   I'm really puzzled. On one of my workstations emacs will no longer load and
run. It used to work just fine. Yesterday, it would not load when X was
running and today it won't load from a console. The error message is:

-bash: /usr/bin/emacs: cannot execute binary file.

   That specific file is a soft link to the real executable. However, all
three emacs* files in /usr/bin on that host have the same permissions as they
do in /usr/bin on another host. And, they all have the same size and
timestamps on the two hosts. Emacs runs just fine on the other host.

   The error message above does not convey to me sufficient information about
why the binary file will not execute. I've had a bunch of things break
without explanation on several machines over the past few weeks and I would
like to put these distractions behind me.

   Any ideas what might cause emacs to not run?

Rich

From seniorr at aracnet.com  Sat Oct 15 15:20:12 2005
From: seniorr at aracnet.com (Russell Senior)
Date: 15 Oct 2005 15:20:12 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID over usb-storage?
Message-ID: <86d5m61kyr.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>


Has anyone done this?  

You can get 2GB SanDisk Cruzer Micros (about an inch long) for a
little over $100 each, plug four of them into a little USB hub and
make a 6 gig RAID5 array.  Light weight, easily transportable, some
some redundancy, built in hot-pluggability, etc.  Hardware cost,
spendy-per-byte, around $450.


-- 
Russell Senior         ``I have nine fingers; you have ten.''
seniorr at aracnet.com

I haven't tried this, but this arstechnica article talks about using
RAID-0 with two thumb drives, with some mixed results.  Sounds like
write performance of larger files was good, read performance wasn't so
amazing.  I don't know if anyone has tried it on Linux.

http://arstechnica.com/reviews/hardware/flash2005.ars/10

Jason

From ed at alcpress.com  Sat Oct 15 23:23:52 2005
From: ed at alcpress.com (Ed Sawicki)
Date: Sat, 15 Oct 2005 23:23:52 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] RAID over usb-storage?
In-Reply-To: <86d5m61kyr.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
References: <86d5m61kyr.fsf@coulee.tdb.com>
Message-ID: <4351F1F8.4060509@alcpress.com>

Russell Senior wrote:
> Has anyone done this?  
> 
> You can get 2GB SanDisk Cruzer Micros (about an inch long) for a
> little over $100 each, plug four of them into a little USB hub and
> make a 6 gig RAID5 array.  Light weight, easily transportable, some
> some redundancy, built in hot-pluggability, etc.  Hardware cost,
> spendy-per-byte, around $450.
> 
> 
I did this about two years ago. I demonstrated it at a BizNix
meeting with 3 or 4 USB flash drives. I did RAID 0, 1, and 5. I
reformatted the drives for ext3. It worked reasonably well but USB 1.0,
at the time, was slow. I never intended it to be practical - I did it
as a way to demonstrate Linux RAID to students. Two of the flash drives
were Lexar Jumpdrives and they failed soon after. I was unable to
determine why they failed - Lexar tech support in India were no
help - in their world computers only run Windows.

Flash drives generally have limited "durability" - about 10 million
write operations. You wouldn't want to use them in applications that
are write intensive.

Ed

From ehem at m5p.com  Sun Oct 16 14:01:20 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Sun, 16 Oct 2005 14:01:20 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] RAID over usb-storage?
In-Reply-To: <20051016190007.7EA8444F7@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200510162101.j9GL1K0R078895@m5p.com>

>From: Russell Senior <seniorr at aracnet.com>
> >>>>> "Ed" == Ed Sawicki <ed at alcpress.com> writes:
> 
> Ed> Flash drives generally have limited "durability" - about 10
> Ed> million write operations. You wouldn't want to use them in
> Ed> applications that are write intensive.
> 
> The potential application was as a lightweight, compact, portable
> backup device.  But kind of expensive.  A DVD burner, if reliable
> enough, would have similar capacity much more inexpensively, although
> possibly much slower too.  As far as I know, you can't rsync to a DVD
> burner! 

DVD+RW media/drives are supposed to be able to do random per-sector
writing, so stick an ext2 filesystem on it, and in theory you should be
able to do so. DVD-RW media/drives are supposed to do per-sector
/re/writing, so should be possible there too.

After that, you're back to the media durability issue though. Optical RW
media has a limited number of writes that can be done.

References: <20051016190007.03DC044F2@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <43529393.10170.91488556@localhost>

On 16 Oct 2005, at 14:17, William Leake wrote:

> IIRC, fakeroot is required to build the .deb package.  You are
> probably looking at the same HOWTO that I followed.  ndiswrapper is
> very picky. I had to remove everything it added and truly start from
> scratch to get it working. At this point, you have probably already
> set it up.  If not, let us know.

Hi!

No, I have not gotten it working again. I did discover an Ubuntu 
page that gave the following instructions --

1. Uninstall the current driver. Use ''ndiswrapper -l'' to get the name 
of the loaded driver, then ''ndiswrapper -e NAME'' to remove the 
currently installed driver.
2. Reinstall the driver with the comment ''ndiswrapper -i 
DRIVER.inf'' using your windows driver.
3. You should now be able to ''modprobe ndiswrapper'' successfully.

I did (1) successfully, but (2) failed because it couldn't find the 
DRIVER.inf file. (My driver is netbc564.) I searched my entire 
filesystem and it's not there. In fact, even netbc*.* turned up 
nothing. I think that (1) does not "remove" the driver; it *deletes* it.

So now I guess I have to go find the driver again. I think I got it 
originally from Linuxant.com. But there was a lot of stuff I had to do 
-- compiling for a "generic" header something-or-another, then 
editing a bunch of files to change "386" to "amd64" or something. 
The instructions are at 

https://wiki.ubuntu.com/HowtoUseNdiswrapperOnAmd64Ubuntu

But they are so poorly written I couldn't get it to work until 
someone on the net held my hand (translation: about 20-30 e-mails 
back and forth). And anyway, I think those instructions may be out 
of date for Breezy.

Oh wait!!! I just remembered -- I have the zip file from Linuxant on 
my Windows computer! I just unzipped it and it became 
bcmwl564.sys and netbc564.inf.

So now I guess I just need to put them back where they belong, 
then follow instructions (2) and (3) above. Any idea where they're 
supposed to live in Breezy?

From pmvw at wbcable.net  Mon Oct 17 12:39:14 2005
From: pmvw at wbcable.net (Piet van Weel)
Date: Mon, 17 Oct 2005 12:39:14 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] PCMCIA wifi recommendations
In-Reply-To: <4353FC10.7020102@comcast.net>
References: <4353FC10.7020102@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <4353FDE2.1000808@wbcable.net>

Interesting question Mel...

I think most of the newer cards can do WPA; however, my personal 
preference is a "Super-G" type of wireless access point. This usually 
doesn't seem to be that big of a deal, but the extra transfer capability 
can be quickly chewed up with adding all the extras on. (WPA, etc.) What 
I would really look around for is a good firmware that has a good 
interface to it. Personally I prefer the D-Link interface to the netgear 
interface; however, that being said it is functional.

Piet van Weel

Subject: [PLUG] 2.6.13.4 Kernel Problems

   I'm not having success running a custom 2.6.13.4 kernel on my Sony Vaio
PCG-V505BC notebook running Slackware-10.2. The kernel and modules build OK,
and the system boots, but it's unusable.

   When a kernel is selected from the lilo menu, three messages appear at the
lower edge of the screen. The last one is:

Loading vmlinuz ..............

   As soon as the dots are done, that screen is replaced with the VESA
framebuffer that has Tux overseeing the display of messages as the system
boots. Except, this does not happen with the 2.6.13.4 kernel. When the lilo
screen is removed, the display is totally blank. I can tell that the system
is booting because of the hard drive activity light. When the light stays off
I know the system is at a login prompt. I blindly log in as root, then issue
the 'reboot' command. The next time I select one of the 2.4 kernels and the
system boots just fine -- with the display working.

   I've rebuilt the kernel at least a half-dozen times, trying various things.
The latest build started with the .config from the working 2.4.24 kernel
provided by EmperorLinux. (I asked them for assistance last Friday and
yesterday but have seen no response from them.) I can send the config file to
anyone who wants it. It's not too large when gzipped.

   I've not before encountered a custom-built kernel that blanked the display
when loading the system. Obviously, this is not a Good Thing and I am at a
loss how to fix it. So are a couple of local gurus who offered suggestions at
Saturday's clinic.

   Knowing that there are a lot of very smart folks on this list who love to
solve complex and obtuse kernel challenges, I'm asking for help. To save
bandwidth in this thread, suggestions on alternate distributions are not
pragmatic. There's no reason why a custom kernel, with the source downloaded
from kernel.org, will not run on any distribution.

   And, once the kernel boots with a visible display, I need to ensure that
sound, acpi, wifi, suspend, and the other portable goodies all work.

Help wanted,

Rich

In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20051018152448.GA21286@rauhaus.org>

* Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> [051018 06:19]:
> 
> Oct 18 05:36:08 salmo inetd[24721]: time/tcp (2): bind: Address already in 
> use
> Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: auth/tcp (2): bind: Address already in 
> use
> Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: pop3/tcp (2): bind: Address already in 
> use
> 
>   Should inetd _not_ be running? I don't use bind here but do have Dan
> Bernstein's dnscache running. I'd appreciate learning what these messages 
> are
> telling me and what I should do to remedy the situation.

Those are referring to bind as in the system call that binds a name to a
socket, and not bind as in the Berkeley Internet Name Daemon.

It most likely means that those services are already running and
listening on the applicable ports, and for some reason they're trying
to be started again and finding that their ports are in use.

--Stafford


From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Tue Oct 18 08:27:20 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Tue, 18 Oct 2005 08:27:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Bind Messages When Bind's Not Running
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840510180827o36c60027ieb6548cf1c022ef@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/18/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    Sunday, I upgraded my server/workstation from Slackware-10.1 to 10.2.
> Everything went smoothly and it has been running just fine since then.
> However, when I look at /ver/log/messages I see many entries such as these,
> repeated throughout the day:
>
> Oct 18 05:36:08 salmo inetd[24721]: time/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
> Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: auth/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
> Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: pop3/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
>
>    Should inetd _not_ be running? I don't use bind here but do have Dan
> Bernstein's dnscache running. I'd appreciate learning what these messages are
> telling me and what I should do to remedy the situation.

That message is not "bind" as in the nameserver, but rather "bind()"
as in the system call assigning an address to a socket.  It means that
inetd is trying to bind to the time, auth, and pop3 ports, but
something is already listening on all three of those ports.  Why that
might be, I have no idea :-)  Perhaps inetd is getting started more
than once?  Perhaps in the upgrade Slackware went from inetd to xinetd
and now both are trying to start?

Jason

>

>
>    Knowing that there are a lot of very smart folks on this list who love to

>
>    And, once the kernel boots with a visible display, I need to ensure that

>

lspci, .config, and lilo.conf?

Hmpf.  I'll bet you had that barb queued up.  Well done, though.

>   I've not before not enabled the console in a kernel, and I'm not sure that
> I've ever seen that as an option. Where is this included in the config menu?

If you type "make menuconfig" it'll be under Device Drivers/Character
Devices/Virtual Terminal and .../Support for Console on VT.  Also
check ...Graphics Support/Console Display Driver Support/VGA text
console.

On 10/18/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
> On Tue, 18 Oct 2005, Jason R. Martin wrote:
>
> >>> # CONFIG_FRAMEBUFFER_CONSOLE is not set
>
> Jason,
>
>    That was it! Hoo-ha!

Heh... love the enthusiasm :-)  Glad it worked out.

>    I did not turn off framebuffer console, so how it became FUBARed I've no
> idea. Thank you very much.

I should hope it is not very common, but sometimes the kernel
developers change the name of a CONFIG_*, and when they do the Kconfig
system doesn't know how to translate, so it assumes the old name has
been removed and the new name has been added.  If you don't catch it
by doing make oldconfig, you'll probably miss it and end up with the
default.  As an example, ask anyone migrating from 2.6.12 and before
to 2.6.13 and later who has Fusion MPT hardware (they even renamed the
modules themselves, screwing up /etc/modprobe.conf).

Jason "what do you mean the device doesn't exist?" Martin


From slinkywizard at integraonline.com  Tue Oct 18 10:57:06 2005
From: slinkywizard at integraonline.com (slinkymax0r)
Date: Tue, 18 Oct 2005 10:57:06 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Global broadband charges
In-Reply-To: <42170.170.135.241.46.1129651994.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
References: <20051018131254.GA5033@patch.com>
	<4.2.2.20051018062532.03d1a9e0@mail.earthlink.net>
	<42170.170.135.241.46.1129651994.squirrel@mail.patch.com>
Message-ID: <30866.64.122.164.5.1129658226.squirrel@64.122.164.5>

>
> Jim Karlock said:
>> At 06:12 AM 10/18/05 , you wrote:
>>>http://www.salon.com/tech/feature/2005/10/18/broadband/index.html
>>>
>>>   In France, you can get super-fast DSL, unlimited phone service and
>>> 100
>>
>> Lack of government (taxpayer) subsidies? Less government regulations?
>
> The article lists several examples where US laws work to inhibit or even
> prohibit competition while the other industrial countries have laws that
> do the opposite.

It's true that the double standard of defining a Telecommunications
service vs. a Information service was a prime example of legislative and
judicial short sightedness.

However, the article's suggested cure for the problem (Government
subsidized service/spectrum overlap etc.) haven't been proven to be any
better in terms of cost or service than the current goofy system.  With
many incumbent carriers rolling out deployments of ADSL2+ and FTTP to many
major markets, the urge to "Do Something!" is probably premature.

The article was also a little fast and loose with the facts regarding
Broadband penetration in relation to Canada, the most comparable nation to
the US in terms of geography.

A Broad Band service is Canada is defined as 64kpbs or higher for the
purposes of estimating broadband penetration. The US government defines it
as 200kpbs. This would explain their higher Broadband penetration rates. 
My bet is their actual highspeed penetration rate (ADSL2+, VDSL, FTTP) is
about the same due to increased backhaul transport bandwidth requirements.


Obviously getting rid of the double standard in terms of the definition of
an "infromation service" is a good first step; But anything beyond that
should probably wait a year until the next broadband hype cycle hits.

It reminds me of Dr. Stranglelove...

Mr. President, we cannot allow a missl^H^H^H broadband gap!


Regards,
Max

Obviously getting rid of the double standard in terms of the definition of
an "infromation service" is a good first step; But anything beyond that
should probably wait a year until the next broadband hype cycle hits.

It reminds me of Dr. Stranglelove...

Mr. President, we cannot allow a missl^H^H^H broadband gap!


Regards,
Max

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Tue Oct 18 12:13:07 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Tue, 18 Oct 2005 12:13:07 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Global broadband charges
In-Reply-To: <20051018131254.GA5033@patch.com>
References: <20051018131254.GA5033@patch.com>
Message-ID: <20051018191307.GA24588@gate.kl-ic.com>

On Tue, Oct 18, 2005 at 06:12:54AM -0700, Michael Rasmussen wrote:
> From:
> http://www.salon.com/tech/feature/2005/10/18/broadband/index.html
> 
>   Free American broadband!
>   
>   In France, you can get super-fast DSL, unlimited phone service and 100
>   TV channels for a mere $38 a month. Why does the same thing cost so much
>   more in the U.S.?
>   
>   By S. Derek Turner
>   
>   Oct. 18, 2005 | Next time you sit down to pay your cable-modem or DSL
>   bill, consider this: Most Japanese consumers can get an Internet
>   connection that's 16 times faster than the typical American DSL line for
>   a mere $22 per month.
>   
>   Across the globe, it's the same story. In France, DSL service that is 10
>   times faster than the typical United States connection; 100 TV channels
>   and unlimited telephone service cost only $38 per month.
>   
> If you don't subscribe to Salon you'll need to get a one day pass by
> viewing a full page ad for something on the way in.

This came up on the personal telco list a while back.  "Most Japanese
Consumers" live in high rises, and provisioning high speed internet
connection (which is mostly a wiring issue) to high rises is far, far
cheaper than provisioning it to spread-out detached suburban dwellings. 

A general rule-of-thumb is that wiring speed for a given level of
copper technology goes down as the square of the distance, and that
the cost of providing a particular speed level goes up as the cube
of the distance.  Optical fiber has the same issues, but they only
kick in at intercontinental range, which is why new high-speed 
interconnects will be optical fiber.  Optical fiber costs are 
instead dominated by end costs and splice costs (it is very difficult
to make a good low-loss splice in the field), so initial deployment
costs are high, and retrofit costs are much higher.

The question becomes "why can't Americans who live in high rises
get better internet connection than those who don't", and that is
probably due to their relatively small percentages and an egalitarian
PUC structure.  Like any country, the mass providers focus regulatory
battles and business efforts on the center of the bell curve, and the
percentage of middle-class-to-rich potential customers living in high
rises in the US is miniscule.

I've never been to France.  In England, 2Mbps DSL, at $40/month, is
typical in urban areas and nonexistent in rural.  In rural Sweden (and
that includes suburban density rural towns) all you can get is dialup. 
In rural suburban-density Japan (at least as of my visit in 2003) all
you can get is dialup as well.  Just what you would expect, given
the densities involved.

In suburban US, new suburban housing areas are frequently getting
connected with optical fiber to the home.  When that becomes
statistically significant, and justifies the regulatory effort,
you can expect interconnect speeds to these new houses to soar -
technically, there are few barriers to speeds in the Gbps range. 
However, the old wire plant that feeds most of existing suburbia
will be replaced very slowly.  This change will occur, probably
over the next 10 years, at the end of which the French will be
back to tearing their hair out and wondering why they lag so far
behind ( minitel redux ).  The Japanese will rip out 20 meters
of 100 base T per customer (to the wiring closet down the hall)
and install 20 meters of fiber, and keep their lead over everyone.

And the Japanese, in turn, will tear their hair out wondering why
they pay twice as much as we do for gas, and ten times as much 
for parking.   Or if they are cyclists, why they can't travel 
those narrow streets without getting run over by a taxi.  The 
infrastructure of every country has its strong and weak points, 
a function of situation and history.

Never attribute to malice what can adequately be explained by 
stupidity.  In this case, the stupidity comes in the shape of
the technically uninformed authors and editors at Salon.

Keith

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Tue Oct 18 12:48:58 2005

Date: Tue, 18 Oct 2005 12:48:58 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] No /proc/acpi
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510181151330.3595@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510181151330.3595@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840510181248j7555bf1fl1a4d7dd122558107@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/18/05, Rich Shepard <rshepard at appl-ecosys.com> wrote:
>    When I grep acpi in the kernel config file I find everything I think I
> want or need turned on. In /proc/kallsyms there are a number of references to
> the battery. But, I don't have a /proc/acpi directory or file.
>
>    Anyone suggest what I missed here?

If the BIOS in your system is older than (by default 2001) ACPI will
be disabled by default, you'll need acpi=force on the kernel command
line.  Check your boot messages (dmesg) for a line like this:

BIOS age (2000) fails cutoff (2001), acpi=force is required to enable ACPI

Jason

From jmknoble+plug at pobox.com  Tue Oct 18 15:42:46 2005
From: jmknoble+plug at pobox.com (Jim Knoble)
Date: Tue, 18 Oct 2005 18:42:46 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] Bind Messages When Bind's Not Running
In-Reply-To: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
References: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510180613130.6196@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
Message-ID: <20051018224246.GC15429@crawfish.ais.com>

Circa 2005-10-18 09:18 dixit Rich Shepard:

: Sunday, I upgraded my server/workstation from Slackware-10.1 to 10.2.
: Everything went smoothly and it has been running just fine since then.
: However, when I look at /ver/log/messages I see many entries such as these,
: repeated throughout the day:
: 
: Oct 18 05:36:08 salmo inetd[24721]: time/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
: Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: auth/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
: Oct 18 05:46:08 salmo inetd[24721]: pop3/tcp (2): bind: Address already in use
: 
: Should inetd _not_ be running? I don't use bind here but do have Dan
: Bernstein's dnscache running. I'd appreciate learning what these
: messages are telling me and what I should do to remedy the situation.

The "bind" in the error messages is not "BIND" (the DNS server), but
rather "bind()" (the system call).  If it were BIND producing the
messages, they would most likely read "named", which is the name of
BIND's DNS cache and DNS server program.

The messages are saying that inetd is trying to set up a socket for
listening on the TCP ports mentioned, but some other process is already
listening on those ports.

You can use 'netstat' to determine what process is listening on those
ports.  Under Linux:

    su
    netstat -tape

(This shows only TCP sockets, with non-numeric portnames/hostnames, and
with extended information which is generally only available to the
superuser).  BSD's netstat uses different syntax.

Alternatively, you can use 'lsof' to get more or different information:

    su
    lsof -i

Best,
jim

jim knoble  |  jmknoble+plug at pobox.com

Subject: [FSFE PR][EN] Early comment on new Microsoft Shared
Source Licenses
Date: Wed, 19 Oct 2005 13:44:07 -0200
From: pr at fsfeurope.org
Reply-To: press at fsfeurope.org
To: press-release at fsfeurope.org

[for immediate release]

FSFE: Early comment on new Microsoft Shared Source Licenses

"Since we so rarely have opportunity to say something positive
about Microsoft, let me begin by congratulating them", says Georg
Greve, president of Free Software Foundation Europe. "Microsoft
finally seems to have made a step forward on their long march
towards giving their users freedom: of the five licenses
published, our cursory first analysis suggests that two of them
indeed fulfill the Free Software Definition."

According to FSFEs first glance, the "Microsoft Permissive
License" (Ms-PL) and "Microsoft Community License" (Ms-CL) both
appear to satisfy the four freedoms that define Free Software. In
particular: The Ms-CL also appears to implement a variation of
the Copyleft idea, which was first implemented by the GNU General
Public License (GPL).

Given previous Microsoft statements about the Copyleft approach
and in particular the GNU GPL as 'viral', 'cancerous' and
'communist', seeing Microsoft now publish licenses applying the
very same principles seems quite an evolution.

Naturally, it is not the publication of licenses, but the
publication of software under a Free Software license, that gives
people freedom:

It is indeed not very useful if every company, administration or
author publishes their own license; so it would have been
preferrable if Microsoft had made the decision to use the GNU
General Public License (GPL) and Lesser General Public License
(LGPL) for its Shared Source program.

Far more than 50% of Free Software worldwide is published under
these licenses, they are very well-known and people trust them
for good reason.

"Microsoft has walked a mile and is now standing mere inches from
the GNU (L)GPL: We fully understand that Microsoft is first
trying to get the nail of its little toe wet in the Free Software
community, and we welcome that," continues Greve. "But in the
course of time we would prefer to see Microsoft join the large
global community of commercial GNU (L)GPL vendors."

"For now it will be good if Microsoft starts relicensing its
portfolio under the Ms-PL or Ms-CL; but we still have to warn
people to be careful about the 'Shared Source' label and look at
the specific licenses: The other three licenses of the Shared
Source program are clearly proprietary and obviously do not
qualify as Free Software." Greve finishes.

The Free Software Foundations will need more time to study all
these licenses and their interactions with other licenses in
depth, so this is not a final evaluation -- and the final
evaluation may as well reveal problems that were not visible at
first sight.

Microsoft still has a long way to go, but for now it seems they
made a step in the right direction, and the Free Software
Foundation Europe hopes they will keep it up.


About the Free Software Foundation Europe:

  The Free Software Foundation Europe (FSFE), founded 2001, is a
  charitable non-governmental organisation dedicated to all
  aspects of Free Software in Europe. Access to software
  determines who may participate in a digital society. The the
  Freedoms to use, copy, modify and redistribute software - as
  described in the Free Software definition - allow equal
  participation in the information age. Creating awareness for
  these issues, securing Free Software politically and legally,
  and giving people Freedom by supporting development of Free
  Software are central issues of the FSFE.

  Further information about FSFE's work can be found at
  http://fsfeurope.org, get active yourself at
  http://fsfeurope.org/contribute/.

https://mail.fsfeurope.org/mailman/listinfo/press-release

From johnxj at comcast.net  Wed Oct 19 20:22:33 2005
From: johnxj at comcast.net (John Jordan)
Date: Wed, 19 Oct 2005 20:22:33 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Adobe Reader 7.0
Message-ID: <4356AB09.15303.A1442B02@localhost>

One of the big reasons I wanted to upgrade my 64-bit Ubuntu 5.04 
to the new Breezy (5.10) was to see if I could install Adobe Reader 
7.0. It wouldn't install under 5.04 -- just aborted with an error 
message (clearly written by Adobe) that it couldn't be installed in a 
64-bit OS. 

Someone here said he had it running on 64-bit Linux using chroot. 
And after googling I discovered that it might be installable without 
chroot as long as the 32-bit libraries are installed.

Well, lo and behold, what did Breezy install? The 32-bit libraries 
are right there! (Missing in Hoary.) So I decided to try again to 
install Reader 7.0. After some figuring out I actually got the 
INSTALL script to run and it appears to be installed. At least, no 
error messages this time.

However, there is a problem. I can't launch it. It did not install a 
launch menu item under Applications. I searched the whole hard 
disk and found what must be the executable --

/usr/local/Adobe/Acrobat7.0/Reader/intellinux/bin/acroread

If I right click on acroread in Konqueror it says it is an executable. I 
tried double-clicking on it, but nada, not even an error message. 
Then I noticed the script had decided it was owned by root, so I 
opened a terminal, su'ed to root, went to intellinux and did chown 
jjj:jjj bin. After that Konqueror showed me as the owner. But it still 
won't launch. And if I type acroread at the command line I just get 
"bash: acroread: command not found." I also went back to /usr and 
tried to chown everything below it to jjj:jjj, but failed to change 
some of them. Googling on chown hasn't enlightened me as to why.

I finally got it installed and it is pissing me off that it won't run. So 
does anyone know how to run it?

Open a terminal window and use the fully qualified path and name.  Make
sure you can at least execute it.  You may need read permissions, too.
Since you were root, you probably got a permissions issue from the X
windowing system session, which is owned by you.  Change the permissions
on the executable so that anyone can execute it.

Ken
CAD 2 CAM

IIRC I downloaded Acrobat Reader 7.0 for Linux and installed it on my SuSE 9.2 
laptop. I dig around and it looks like the "acroread" program is a shell 
script so make sure you have the file marked as executable "chmod 755" which 
should allow any user to execute it. Assuming the shell script is the same or 
similar to what I have make sure you have a /bin/sh executable as well since 
the script calls that out at the top.

Tony

From evan at evanheidtmann.info  Wed Oct 19 21:31:08 2005
From: evan at evanheidtmann.info (Evan Heidtmann)
Date: Thu, 20 Oct 2005 00:31:08 -0400
Subject: [PLUG] Adobe Reader 7.0
In-Reply-To: <4356B761.6628.A1746429@localhost>
References: <4356AB09.15303.A1442B02@localhost>
	<4356B761.6628.A1746429@localhost>
Message-ID: <1129782668.11958.25.camel@luffy>

On Wed, 2005-10-19 at 21:15 -0700, John Jordan wrote:
> Thanks to you and Rob. I'm making progress. I used the fully 
> qualified path (never heard that term before, but I figured it out) -- 
> and got "error while loading shared libraries libBIB.so: cannot open 
> sharedn object file: no such file or directory."

That doesn't sound like a permissions issue to me. It means that the
libBIB.so library can't be found. Does this file exist on your system?
On mine, it's in /usr/lib/Adobe/Acrobat7.0/Reader/intellinux/lib/

> Sounds like a permissions issue. I know a bit about chown and 
> that's it. In Konqeror it shows the permissions for the acroread file 
> as rwxr-xr-x and the owner as me. But there are lots of other 
> folders under the Adobe folder, and I was unable to chown all of 
> them to me (command didn't take, for some reason).

You shouldn't need to own the files in order to use them. Those
permissions give everyone (including you) execute permission, regardless
of the owner of the file.

> I'm the owner of this computer and the only one who will ever use 
> it. In fact, if I ever lost it, I'd just as soon no one else can get into it, 
> at least not without wiping everything out and installing Windows 
> on it. So can I just make myself the owner of everything from the 
> root on up? And give myself permission for everything at the same 
> time?

That sounds like a bad idea. I imagine a number of system programs would
be rather confused if you did that.

-Evan

It sounds like the dynamic linker doesn't know where the library is. 
Try adding /usr/lib/Adobe/Acrobat7.0/Reader/intellinux/lib to
/etc/ld.so.conf and then run ldconfig.  Then see if you can run
acroread.

> > You shouldn't need to own the files in order to use them. Those
> > permissions give everyone (including you) execute permission,
> > regardless of the owner of the file.
>
> I think the problem is that I must have run the INSTALL script as
> root. I should have run it as jjj. On the other hand, doesn't rwxr-xr-x
> mean everyone can execute the file? If so, then I don't know what
> the problem is, because all the files in /usr/local/Adobe have the
> same rwxr-xr-x permissions. If that means everyone has read-write-
> execute permissions, then you must be right -- it is not a
> permissions thing.

Right, typically you would want root to do the install into a
system-wide directory, and you want root to own the file so nobody
else can write to it, such as replacing acroread with a program that
sends all your credit card info to an email address and then makes fun
of yo mamma.

> Still confused.

I'm always confused, it's kinda fun :-)

Jason

only run for cover it's just a waste of time." - Live

From krisa at subtend.net  Thu Oct 20 14:01:21 2005
From: krisa at subtend.net (Kris)
Date: Thu, 20 Oct 2005 14:01:21 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] some wireless work
Message-ID: <20051020210120.GA26576@subtend.net>

I hit a wall, and now need some help in order to get this deployed quickly.  I 
have two scenario's.

I will be using two wrt54gs's running openwrt.

Scenario A: Repeater
--------------------
PSU uses all Cisco 1231's.  I need this wrt54gs to extend coverage for a 
Cisco.

switch ----cat5---- cisco1231 ----b/g---- wrt54gs ----b/g---- wireless-client 

So I would be needing the wrt to associate to the cisco, and repeate the 
wireless signal back to clients.  I tried WDS with no luck, but could be 
missing something.

Scenario B: Wired captive portal
--------------------------------
Disable the wireless on the wrt.  Setup either Nocat or Chillispot to 
captive portal the LAN side to a Radiator server on the WAN side

A Cisco1231 will connect to the LAN side with an Open ssid to be captured 
by the portal running on the wrt.

switch ----cat5---- (wan)wrt54g(lan) ----cat5---- cisco1231

I'm looking to do this ASAP.  Send estimates and availability to 
krisa at pdx.edu (if someone wants to forward this to the PTP list, feel free 
to do so).

-- 
I'm just a packet pusher.

wifes computer for $280 shipped last week.

The ones I have are the 1905FP UltraSharp.

    ABBOTT AND COSTELLO IN 2005

You (sort of!) have to be old enough to remember Abbott and Costello, and too
old to REALLY understand computers, to fully appreciate this.

For those of us who sometimes get flustered by our computers, please read
on... for those who don't, you are too young anyway.

If Bud Abbott and Lou Costello were alive today, their famous sketch, "Who's
  on first?" might have turned out something like this:


    COSTELLO CALLS TO BUY A COMPUTER FROM ABBOTT

    ABBOTT: Super Duper computer store. Can I help you?
    COSTELLO: Thanks. I'm setting up an office in my den and I'm thinking
    about buying a computer.

    ABBOTT: Mac?
    COSTELLO: No, the name's Lou.

    ABBOTT: Your computer?
    COSTELLO: I don't own a computer. I want to buy one.

    ABBOTT: Mac?
    COSTELLO: I told you, my name's Lou.

    ABBOTT: What about Windows?
    COSTELLO: Why? Will it get stuffy in here?

    ABBOTT: Do you want a computer with Windows?
    COSTELLO: I don't know. What will I see when I look at the windows?

    ABBOTT: Wallpaper.
    COSTELLO: Never mind the windows. I need a computer and software.

    ABBOTT: Software for Windows?
    COSTELLO: No. On the computer! I need something I can use to write
    proposals, track expenses and run my business. What do you have?

    ABBOTT: Office.
    COSTELLO: Yeah, for my office Can you recommend anything?

    ABBOTT: I just did.
    COSTELLO: You just did what?

    ABBOTT: Recommend something.
    COSTELLO: You recommended something?

    ABBOTT: Yes.
    COSTELLO: For my office?

    ABBOTT: Yes.
    COSTELLO: OK, what did you recommend for my office?

    ABBOTT: Office.
    COSTELLO: Yes, for my office!

    ABBOTT: I recommend Office with Windows.
    COSTELLO: I already have an office with windows! OK, let's just say I'm
    sitting at my computer and I want to type a proposal. What do I need?

    ABBOTT: Word.
    COSTELLO: What word?

    ABBOTT: Word in Office.
    COSTELLO: The only word in office is office.

    ABBOTT: The Word in Office for Windows.
    COSTELLO: Which word in office for windows?

    ABBOTT: The Word you get when you click the blue  "W".
    COSTELLO: I'm going to click your blue "w" if you don't start with some
    straight answers. OK, forget that. Can I watch movies on the Internet?

    ABBOTT: Yes, you want Real One.
    COSTELLO: Maybe a real one, maybe a cartoon. What I watch is none of your
    business. Just tell me what I need!

    ABBOTT: Real One.
    COSTELLO: If it's a long movie, I also want to watch reels 2, 3 and 4. Can
    I watch them?

    ABBOTT: Of course.
    COSTELLO: Great! With what?

    ABBOTT: Real One.
    COSTELLO: OK, I'm at my computer and I want to watch a movie. What do I do?

    ABBOTT: You click the blue "1".
    COSTELLO: I click the blue one what?

    ABBOTT: The blue "1".
    COSTELLO: Is that different from the blue w?

    ABBOTT: The blue "1" is Real One and the blue "W" is Word.
    COSTELLO: What word?

    ABBOTT: The Word in Office for Windows.
    COSTELLO: But there are three words in "office for windows"!

    ABBOTT: No, just one. But it's the most popular Word in the world.
    COSTELLO: It is?

    ABBOTT: Yes, but to be fair, there aren't many other Words left.
    It pretty much wiped out all the other Words out there.
    COSTELLO: And that word is real one?

    ABBOTT: Real One has nothing to do with Word. Real One isn't even part of
    Office.
    COSTELLO: STOP! Don't start that a gain. What about financial bookkeeping?
    You have anything I can track my money with?

    ABBOTT: Money.
    COSTELLO: That's right. What do you have?

    ABBOTT: Money.
    COSTELLO: I need money to track my money?

    ABBOTT: It comes bundled with your computer.
    COSTELLO: What's bundled with my computer?

    ABBOTT: Money
    COSTELLO: Money comes with my computer?

    ABBOTT: Yes. No extra charge.
    COSTELLO: I get a bundle of money with my computer? How much?

    ABBOTT: One copy.
    COSTELLO: Isn't it illegal to copy money?

    ABBOTT: Microsoft gave us a license to copy Money.
    COSTELLO: They can give you a license to copy  money?

    ABBOTT: Why not? THEY OWN IT!

    (A few days later)

    ABBOTT: Super Duper computer store. Can I help  you?
    COSTELLO: How do I turn my computer off?

   ABBOTT: Click on "START".......

Others replied:
> Don't forget to look at Costco, I just bought one of their Princeton ...
> I also have a 19" Princeton LCD from Costco (mail-order, even!), and ...
> People say what they will, but I have three Dell 19" (model 1905FP) ...

Uh, at the risk of seeming pedantic, please change the subject line
when changing the subject.  I am looking for TEST IMAGES, not monitor
brand suggestions.  After a bit of looking already, with six images on
a USB thumb drive, I assure you that different monitors have widely
varying image quality.  Some appear identically good, but might
differentiate on special images I do not have.  That is why I want
test images from elsewhere, to select the best from the good.  

I want video bandwidth tests, grayscale tests, etc.  The article at
   http://www.xbitlabs.com/articles/other/display/lcd-guide.html
details some of the issues involved.  I have done professional work
with LCD displays, and have worked with professional test images;
however, those images belong to my clients and so I need an "open
source" set.

A screenshot of 4 xterms, presented to the monitor as HD15 analog,
was the most demanding test in my homebrew test set.  Looked pretty
good on a Viewsonic, pretty crappy on a Hyundai.  While I suspect
the Hyundai has a sampling rate tweak that might fix the picture, 
I want a monitor that can figure this out automatically.

If I can't look at the monitor in the store, with test images I
select, I don't really care what kind of bargain price I can get. 
The computer the monitor will connect to, a Shuttle miniATX, only
produces HD15 analog, so unfortunately DVI quality is not applicable.

----------------------------------------------------------
All that said, can anyone suggest a source of test images?
----------------------------------------------------------

Keith

Message-ID: <1129730452.11958.15.camel@luffy>

On Tue, 2005-10-18 at 13:19 -0700, Rich Shepard wrote:
>    Is there a translator from milliwatt-hours to time that can be applied to
> /proc/acpi/battery/BAT1/state?

((BAT1 remaining capacity) + (BAT0 remaining capacity)) 
	/ (present rate) = time in hours

I know of no simple application that does just this. The present
discharge rate fluctuates so much that a mental estimate is generally
good enough.

-Evan

milliwatt-hours / milliwatts = hours

Rob

Hi everybody, I am having some issues with IO on my mythtv box (Fedora 3,
not the latest kernel). While watching certain programs skipping ahead 30
seconds can be quite slow, like 5 seconds, and jumping ahead 10 minutes may
take 3-4 minutes. I have been watching top and using iostat to get some more
information about what the bottleneck might be. Top shows a high CPU iowait
percentage, around 70-80%, during the delay. Also iostat shows a lot of wait
also. I thought that perhaps defragmenting the partition would help, but it
has not really done much, yet. I have noticed some improvement, but the
problem has not gone away. The filesystem where the programs are stored is
XFS. Any help or suggestions are appreciated.
Thanks,

Rob Anderson
riznob at gmail.com

"... CSSED a GTK2 application to help create and maintain CSS style sheets
for web developing.

editing. It is an Open Source project, it means that you can download the
program but also its source.

"It features syntax highlighting, syntax validation, MDI notebook based
interface, quick CSS properties and values insertion, auto-completion and
dialog-based insertion of CSS complex values.

"Iago Rubio - CSSED author and project administrator."

themselves to DW.

In defense of Nvu - I had used Dreamweaver for several years, was never  
entirely happy with it despite its extensive capabilities. A few months  
ago I switched to Nvu (partly on Windows, partly on Linux). And I like it,  
in some ways prefer it to Dreamweaver. Its functionality is not quite as  
extensive as Dreamweaver's, and the best way to handle CSS remains text  
coding (but then, I usually resorted to that in Dreamweaver too). It does  
have good WYSIWYG/code switching tools. Nvu may have some distance yet to  
travel, but I can recommend it for general web site editing.

I think of NVu as the best open source editor when you're primarily 
interested in a WYSIWIG visual modus operandi. If you're more interested 
in active websites with lots of code (PHP, Ruby/Rails, Javascript, etc.) 
I think Bluefish is a better choice. I prefer NVu to Quanta, by the way.

Yes. DMA is enabled.

$ hdparm /dev/hda

/dev/hda:
multcount = 16 (on)
IO_support = 0 (default 16-bit)
unmaskirq = 0 (off)
using_dma = 1 (on)
keepsettings = 0 (off)
readonly = 0 (off)
readahead = 256 (on)
geometry = 65535/16/63, sectors = 80026361856, start = 0

On Sun, 23 Oct 2005, Charlie Schluting wrote:

> Sounds like the FSB and/or clock multiplier are incorrect, i.e. you're
> overclocking it :)

Charlie,

   No, it's not overclocked by design or error. The bloody motherboard has
failed. Here're the details:

   It's the VIA KT600 chipset on this board, and the DDR 400MHz RAM is
correct. My other system has the KT333 chipset on an MSI motherboard and
works just fine.

   The Soyo manual has a trouble-shooting appendix. In the section on the
system locking up they suggest setting the "fail-safe" options in the BIOS.
Well, I did that and now the system neither boots nor lets me back into the
BIOS to change the settings. All I get is the (ahem!) "pretty" Soyo logo on
the full screen, a bit of hard drive activity, then nothing. Reset gets me
back to the same place.

   I'm more than a bit annoyed. I'll call Soyo tomorrow, but I suspect the
board has failed on me. And, I never got my rebates on it, either.

Rich

From keithl at kl-ic.com  Mon Oct 24 11:16:48 2005
From: keithl at kl-ic.com (Keith Lofstrom)
Date: Mon, 24 Oct 2005 11:16:48 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] DVI, PCI, Linux
Message-ID: <20051024181648.GA13058@gate.kl-ic.com>


Well, I bought the nice 19 inch LCD monitor (A Viewsonic VA912B,
runner-up was a Samsung 930V, and no, Costco no longer has Princeton
and I no longer buy from Dell) and brought it home and hooked it
up to the built-in HD15 video on the Shuttle ST62K microATX I plan
to use with it.  It does not look as good as at the store - the
built-in VGA on the Shuttle has less-than-perfect bandwidth, and
the worst case test image (alternating rows of black and white
pixels) looks a little streaky.  This indicates that the sampler
in the Viewsonic is not sharply selecting the center of the pixels.
The streaky is not bad, but it also results in text characters
that are not as sharp as they should be.

The ST62K does have one PCI slot, which I can sacrifice to the
gods of sharp video.  I will not be gaming or running videos on
the box, so I can live with slow video.  I can put a DVI (digital
video interface) card in there, and drive the monitor that way,
and get maximum sharpness.  My wife, who I am building the system
for, says "it is good enough already", but if she is to support 
me in my old age I can't have her going blind ...

---------------------------------------------------------------
So, the question:  What currently available PCI (not AGP) video
cards support DVI and Linux?  I would love something with open
source drivers and super-duper performance, but I will settle
for binary drivers and 2D functional and cheap.
---------------------------------------------------------------

Keith

P.S.  I am running Fedora Core 4 and xorg on the box - I assume
all the drivers are available on the CD or from up2date/yum .

On Mon, 24 Oct 2005, Keith Lofstrom wrote:

> So, the question:  What currently available PCI (not AGP) video 
> cards support DVI and Linux?  I would love something with open 
> source drivers and super-duper performance, but I will settle for 
> binary drivers and 2D functional and cheap.

You used to be able to get PCI cards based on the ATI Radeon RV100 
(aka Radeon 7000) set. If they're still available, avoid them, at 
least for now. There's a severe X.Org bug that renders them useless 
for anything but text mode. See the Red Hat bugzilla entry for info:

   https://bugzilla.redhat.com/bugzilla/show_bug.cgi?id=170008

When it worked, however, the 7000 combined with the X.Org radeon 
driver was pretty good...

Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

From ehem at m5p.com  Mon Oct 24 16:23:12 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Mon, 24 Oct 2005 16:23:12 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] DVI, PCI, Linux
In-Reply-To: <20051024190005.6CF0144D2@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200510242323.j9ONNCgN001911@m5p.com>

>From: Keith Lofstrom <keithl at kl-ic.com>
> So, the question:  What currently available PCI (not AGP) video
> cards support DVI and Linux?  I would love something with open
> source drivers and super-duper performance, but I will settle
> for binary drivers and 2D functional and cheap.

Pretty tall order, DVI has been getting more common, but even now DVI
isn't universal for all graphics chip outputs. New graphics chips are
tending to have AGP or PCIe connections, not PCI.

As mentioned on the last graphics chip thread, you've got two options, an
ATi chip or an nVIDIA chip. You /might/ just find a card with a Matrox
chip that can accomodate your desires as well. Support will not be an
issue, you'll have the choice of closed-source and super capable, or
open-source and merely capable.

The problem will be finding a PCI card with a DVI card. Your most likely
candidates will be cards with nVIDIA MX440 or MX4000 chips. Their 3D
performance is unimpressive now, but they're capable chips and they're
recent enough that you'll have reasonable odds of finding one with a DVI
output.

In-Reply-To: <1130297040.26303.234.camel@localhost.localdomain>

Vram wrote:
> Hi
> 
> I finally downloaded minicom and set it up..
> 
> And nothing happen..

And to mention something I saw earlier.. there's no special cisco 
cables. Some devices require a nullmodem cable, but that's not special. 
if you have serial->cat5 converters, the easy thing to do is check to 
see if it says "console" or "terminal."

Most devices talk @ 9600 with 8N1. or 7N1. I've seen some storage arrays 
that require 38400.. but that's the exception.

Google this: site:cisco.com connecting serial port
The first webpage explains step by step.

If your question is just about minicom, see the manpage, or use CTRL-A Z 
to access the help.. it's pretty easy, just hit 'o' to configure, then 
select serial port settings.

-Charlie

As I read the article I realize my mistake...

Com1 is ttyS0...  So that is why it didn't work...

Also Cisco Routers/Switches use a role-over cable...

So from com1  you need a DB9/25 to RJ45 adapter. Then a role-over cable
to the console port..

<A role-over cable has pin 1 to 8, 2 to 7, 3 to 6, etc,>

That's what I was referring to. I know you can use normal adapters with 
a crossover cat5 cable too :)

Most newer cisco's use a RJ45 connector that take a regular patch cable 
and "terminal" serial adapter, or the other with a crossover cable. It's 
all dependant on what the device think it is in terms of serial 
communication: a modem or a terminal.

Nice article. There's a link in your article to

to build a special cable would have to work that out.

***The Real Problem with Textbooks: A SafariU Editorial 
The Government Accountability Office (GAO) reports that textbook prices
rose 186 percent from 1986 to 2004--twice the rate of inflation. This
populist revolt is getting lots of press, but the problem with textbooks
is deeper and more fundamental than high prices: expensive textbooks are
relics of the pre-internet world. O'Reilly CIO CJ Rayhill explicates and
highlights O'Reilly's web-based co-publishing tool: SafariU.
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/10/21/problem-with-textbooks-safariu-editorial.html

***EuroOSCON--Doctorow on Europe's Coming Broadcast Flag 
In Cory Doctorow's closing keynote at O'Reilly's first-ever European
Open Source Convention, he stated, "Right now we find ourselves in the
midst of a global attack on free software. A global attack that is
supposed to uphold copyright, but that has no nexus with what copyright
actually does." At the heart of this attack, asserts Cory, is the DRM
and certain American entertainment companies' push for a Broadcast Flag
in Europe. Daniel Steinberg reports on Cory's keynote.
http://www.oreillynet.com/pub/a/network/2005/10/21/doctorow-on-europe-broadcast-flag.html


***What Is Ruby on Rails?
Ruby on Rails is an impressive web development framework that will soon
reach version 1.0. While there's a lot of buzz, it can sometimes be
difficult to discern the steak beneath the sizzle. Curt Hibbs walks
through the features and pieces of Ruby on Rails to show how it fits
together and where its big benefits come from.
http://www.onlamp.com/pub/a/onlamp/2005/10/13/what_is_rails.html

From aschlemm at comcast.net  Wed Oct 26 23:24:23 2005
From: aschlemm at comcast.net (Tony Schlemmer)
Date: Wed, 26 Oct 2005 23:24:23 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Best hardware
In-Reply-To: <435FBB1F.5090600@comcast.net>
References: <435FBB1F.5090600@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <200510262324.23435.aschlemm@comcast.net>

On Wednesday 26 October 2005 10:21, Dirk Ouellette wrote:
> May I have some ideas on the quietest, most reliable hardware, as in the
> harddrive, fan [s], motherboard, and maybe a flat screen 17" to 19"
> monitor under $1,000 not counting the monitor.? Should I move to  64 bit
> ? I'm tired of the noisy high pitched whines.
> Thanks, Dirk
> _______________________________________________
> PLUG mailing list
> PLUG at lists.pdxlinux.org
> http://lists.pdxlinux.org/mailman/listinfo/plug

I went through this a month ago when I replaced my old tower system. The power 
supply and 40mm chipset fan were so loud and I don't know how I put up with 
all that noise for 4 1/2 years.  I got a huge Zalman copper heatsink with a 
120MM fan that is variable speed. I also got a 480W Thermaltake Silent 
Purepower power supply that has an adjustable fan on it and which allows me 
to lower the speed of one of its fans. 

By far the most noise is the power supply if the fan speed is at 100%. With a 
120MM fan on the CPU cooler I don't really hear much difference in noise 
between low and high speed so I tend to just run the CPU fan at nearly full 
speed.

I didn't spare any expense for heat sinks and got copper heat spreaders for my 
PC3200 DIMMs as well. I've seen some memory that includes the heat spreaders 
but the Kingston memory I got didn't have them so I added them. 

About the only thing I notice is that my mirrored SATA-150 are not silent when 
the head seek mechinism activates but they SATA drives are much quieter than 
the my old 10K RPM Ultra160 SCSI drives I had in my old system.

I was also tired of having a heavy tower case made out of steel and so I also 
splurged for a Lian Li aluminum case. It's noticably ligher but that monster 
Zalman heatsink I got really adds some weight. The bright blue power LED on 
the Lian LI case makes a great nightlight in TV room. I cannot believe how 
bright it is. :-D

Tony

-- 
Anthony Schlemmer
aschlemm at comcast.net

From gilmanhunt at comcast.net  Thu Oct 27 11:36:19 2005
From: gilmanhunt at comcast.net (gilmanhunt at comcast.net)
Date: Thu, 27 Oct 2005 18:36:19 +0000
Subject: [PLUG] missing /etc/localtime ?
Message-ID: <102720051836.29991.43611E230007D36C0000752722007610649B029A08020E03040709@comcast.net>

On one of our servers (UnitedLinux 1.0), when we reboot the system, the /etc/localtime symlink (or file) vanishes. I'm not sure where in the process it goes away.  I set into /etc/init.d/boot.local a line to symlink the file from the original in /usr/share/zoneinfo/america, but that's an imperfect fix because some services that are already running need to be restarted to get the right time stamps (mysql, sendmail, etc , although syslogd has the correct time).

Any ideas on why it's vanishing, or how to correct it?

Grepping for localtime in /etc/rc.d/ (recursive) doesn't yield any promising results; just the boot.local entry.

-Russ

From nsxfreddy at gmail.com  Thu Oct 27 12:05:20 2005
From: nsxfreddy at gmail.com (Jason R. Martin)
Date: Thu, 27 Oct 2005 12:05:20 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] any reasons not to install a 64 bit specific distribution?
In-Reply-To: <20051027183444.1B1508F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
References: <20051027183444.1B1508F03@tinman.seitzassoc.com>
Message-ID: <c29537840510271205k2e767296o7c1b179247d17d65@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/27/05, Galen Seitz <galens at seitzassoc.com> wrote:
>
> Due to the requirements of some design tools I need to run, I now have
> an Athlon 64 box sitting here.  I plan to install Centos on it.  Are
> there any reasons not to install the x86_64 version?

Most of the x86_64 problems that I know of are either plugin problems
for the 64-bit Firefox (such as Flash, which you might find you don't
miss after all) or related to greyhat multimedia apps (such as the
w32codecs for mplayer/xine/etc).  As long as any apps you need to run
are either pure 32-bit or pure 64-bit I think you won't really notice
the difference from a user perspective.  You can work around the above
problems by installing the 32-bit versions of
Firefox/mplayer/xine/etc, but things like automagic updates (yum) will
get hairy.

Also, hacks like ndiswrapper can be a problem if there's no 64-bit
Windows driver for your wireless.  The only one I know of that works
is the Broadcom driver (what I have).

Just for balance-sake, a good question to ask yourself is if there's
any reason why you *should* install x86_64, such as >4GB memory or
apps that will take (noticable) advantage of the extra general purpose
registers and enhanced instruction set.

Jason "x86_64 FC4" Martin

From russj at dimstar.net  Thu Oct 27 15:42:05 2005
From: russj at dimstar.net (Russ Johnson)
Date: Thu, 27 Oct 2005 15:42:05 -0700
Subject: [PLUG] Best hardware
In-Reply-To: <435FBB1F.5090600@comcast.net>
References: <435FBB1F.5090600@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <436157BD.9050902@dimstar.net>

Dirk Ouellette wrote:

> May I have some ideas on the quietest, most reliable hardware, as in 
> the harddrive, fan [s], motherboard, and maybe a flat screen 17" to 
> 19" monitor under $1,000 not counting the monitor.? Should I move to  
> 64 bit ? I'm tired of the noisy high pitched whines. 

As others have mentioned, bigger fans move as much or more air at lower 
rpms. The noise you hear is from the tips of the blades cutting through 
the air. If the fan is going slower, there's less noise.

I made sure all fans in my system were at least 80mm. The case fan is 
120mm.

I didn't get any, but I've also seen fans that were driven with magnets 
around the perimeter of the housing, not at the hub. All blades were 
attached to each other at the tip to eliminate the tip noise.

I know my new system is much quieter than my old one. Now to quiet down 
my server rack... :/

Russ

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Thu Oct 27 18:02:13 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Thu, 27 Oct 2005 18:02:13 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Now for something different: linux software!
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510271755290.6548@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   I know that some of you use LaTeX/LyX for your writing. I've been looking
for a better 2D vector graphics editor that tgif; that's sorta' OK, but the
text is not up to TeX standards. So, today I downloaded, installed, and
tested three of the dozen-plus that are available: jpicedt, ipe, and dia.

   For my test diagrams, the first two are really great because their native
format is .eps or .pdf and they compile directly into a .dvi or .pdf file.
The text, of course, is outstanding. 'Dia' is supposed to be a visio clone,
and I'm not sure how to take its output for use in a LaTeX document or beamer
slide presentation.

   jpicedt is written in java; ipe in C++ (with Qt as the UI toolkit). They
both took a bit of trial and error to learn their ways, but I really like
both and would be hard pressed to select one over the other. Both are highly
recommended. If I find some cute clipart images that work in dia, I'll
probably use that one, too.

Hope that someone finds this useful,

Rich

From ehem at m5p.com  Fri Oct 28 13:40:01 2005
From: ehem at m5p.com (Elliott Mitchell)
Date: Fri, 28 Oct 2005 13:40:01 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] any reasons not to install a 64 bit specific distribution?
In-Reply-To: <20051028081202.9420944BC@ulke.nakedape.cc>
Message-ID: <200510282040.j9SKe1E4079071@m5p.com>

>From: "Jason R. Martin" <nsxfreddy at gmail.com>
> Just for balance-sake, a good question to ask yourself is if there's
> any reason why you *should* install x86_64, such as >4GB memory or
> apps that will take (noticable) advantage of the extra general purpose
> registers and enhanced instruction set.

>4GB may be the line where you need 64-bit address space *period*, but it
becomes worthwhile before then. In particular you run into the issue of
the shared kernel address space versus user address space. Perhaps I'm
used to 2.4, but the kernel always grabbed a minimum of 1GB of address
space. There is also the issue that the Unix paradigm for code is that it
is always available (notably startup/shutdown code), and you simply swap
out those portions, rather than bothering to unmap them. This also serves
to clutter address space.

Due to these factors I'd tend to place the line at 2GB. Any more and
you're in danger of encountering one of these.

  It's not really broken.  I just won't compile.  :-)

  My understanding is that the RedHat licenced offerings include
non-GPL secret-sauce in the kernel.  The non-GPL files are removed
when the GPL portions of the source are posted on their web site.  The
GPL code won't compile without the non-GPL code or extensive code
cleanup.

The folks at White Box Linux may have a replacement kernel that compiles.

http://www.whiteboxlinux.org/

This pulls the file a line at a time:

for EACH in `cat filenamehere`; do
    process line as needed
done

I lifted the lid on that worm can and quickly closed it again! I took a 
deep breath and looked again, and now I know why I just use Thunderbird 
with the standard Comcast e-mail account. Perhaps, someday, I'll have 
time to dig into this more deeply.

Rich Shepard wrote:

>   The problem is that Dick doesn't run his own MTA so he can't check the 
> RBLs
> on incoming mail ... as far as I know.

Is there some way to set myself up to run a MTA and still use 
Thunderbird and Comcast? Or do I need to switch to a different e-mail 
client (and fiddle with stuff I haven't taken the time to read up on 
related to Unix mail systems) to be able to do that? From the brief 
perusal I gave the SpamAssassin site last night, it appears that I can't 
use it with my current setup.

>   The RBLs I have checking incoming mail finds some IP addresses in all the
> major ISPs world wide, including comcast.net, verizon.net, and a bunch in
> Europe.

As I understand it, Comcast is running some kind of spam filtering for 
me, and it does catch a fair amount of stuff, storing it in my space on 
their server for some period of time and then deleting it. IIRC, I can 
chose to have this filtering on or off, but don't have any way to tweak 
the settings. I wish they offered a bit more, but I guess that would be 
too much for them to administer.

I have friends on SpiritOne who have access to their own black lists and 
white lists. It would be nice if Comcast were to add even that much. I'd 
consider switching, but we're too far out in the country to get a high 
speed connection from anyone other than Comcast.

Thanks to both of you for your ideas.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sat Oct 29 12:01:20 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sat, 29 Oct 2005 12:01:20 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Weird e-mail
In-Reply-To: <4363A66A.9050004@comcast.net>
References: <4362A7AE.40609@comcast.net> <4362B308.2020508@dimstar.net>
	<Pine.LNX.4.63.0510281701260.6993@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>
	<4363A66A.9050004@comcast.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510291136420.29343@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Sat, 29 Oct 2005, Richard C. Steffens wrote:

> Is there some way to set myself up to run a MTA and still use Thunderbird
> and Comcast? Or do I need to switch to a different e-mail client (and
> fiddle with stuff I haven't taken the time to read up on related to Unix
> mail systems) to be able to do that? From the brief perusal I gave the
> SpamAssassin site last night, it appears that I can't use it with my
> current setup.

Dick,

   Yes and no; but there are cable subscription subtleties that others need to
address. Here's the short story on Internet mail.

   There are four major categories of software involved:

   1.) Retrieval from a remote server (I don't know the formal name for this
category). 'fetchmail' is the most usually used in the linux world for this.
The protocols involved are POP3 (Post Office Protocol) and IMAP (Internet
Message Access Protocol). You are probably using one of these protocols to
collect your mail from Comcast. If you run your own server you don't need
this on a local machine, but you need POP3 or IMAP on other hosts.

   2.) The mail server, more formally the Mail Transport Agent (MTA; and not
what The Kingston Trio song glorifies). This implements the SMTP (Simple Mail
Transport Protocol) that takes an incoming message and determines where to
send it next. Examples of MTAs include sendmail, postfix, exim, qmail, and
smail. If you install and configure the MTA, you can filter incoming mail and
reject the spam before the full message is sent; the decisions are based on
the initial conversation between the remote server (the client) and your
server. This takes you off Comcast's mail server which may not be permitted
by their contract.

   3.) The Local Delivery Agent (LDA), usually procmail. When the server
accepts an incoming message because the next step is in the local network,
it passes the message on to the LDA for local delivery to the proper mailbox.
More filtering can occur here; usually assigning the mail to the proper
recipeient and appropriate "folder" or file based on selection criteria.
SpamAssassin usually sits between the MTA and the LDA.

   4.) The Mail User Agent (MUA), more commonly called the mail client or mail
reader. Thunderbird, Sylpheed, Kmail, pine, mutt, elm, and a few dozen others
are all available for use.

   Incoming mail uses categories 2-4, and sometimes 1. Outgoing mail uses
categories 4 and 2.

   The mail system is analagous to a family dinner at your favorite Chinese
restaurant: pick one from Column A, one from Column B, one from Column C, and
one from Column D. You can mix and match to your heart's content.

   So, you could certainly continue to use Thunderbird as your MUA, regardless
of how mail goes in or out. Do you want to learn the subtleties of
installing, configuring, and maintaining your own server? Only if the
incoming spam is sufficiently annoying that you find the time and effort
worth while. Some of us do, many others don't.

> As I understand it, Comcast is running some kind of spam filtering for me,
> and it does catch a fair amount of stuff, storing it in my space on their
> server for some period of time and then deleting it. IIRC, I can chose to
> have this filtering on or off, but don't have any way to tweak the
> settings. I wish they offered a bit more, but I guess that would be too
> much for them to administer.

   Tune Thunderbird. I've not looked into that because when Pamela complains
about spam I take care of it system-wide.

> I have friends on SpiritOne who have access to their own black lists and
> white lists. It would be nice if Comcast were to add even that much. I'd
> consider switching, but we're too far out in the country to get a high
> speed connection from anyone other than Comcast.

   And there's the rub. There was an interesting thread on the postfix mail
list a week or so ago on this very topic. In Europe, apparently, the goal is
to provide high bandwidth connections to everyone via the telephone systems.
In this country regulations have been enacted to keep the various interests
(not the consumers', of course) well protected. In other words, politics as
usual. And that is not an invitation for a flame thread, just a statement of
reality.

Rich

From heinlein at madboa.com  Sat Oct 29 18:20:44 2005
From: heinlein at madboa.com (Paul Heinlein)
Date: Sat, 29 Oct 2005 18:20:44 -0700 (PDT)
Subject: [PLUG] Bash script w/external data
In-Reply-To: <43639A72.9040303@dimstar.net>
References: <f441a6240510281803p44ddd8c2v6e98972ae61c3e0e@mail.gmail.com>
	<43639A72.9040303@dimstar.net>
Message-ID: <Pine.OSX.4.64.0510291812080.2728@arrowhead.local>

On Sat, 29 Oct 2005, Russ Johnson wrote:

> pdxfiles wrote:
>
>> Is there a way for bash to pull the data line by line, or is 
>> another tool needed? I couldn't seem to find anything that I could 
>> specify a line to read. I think I could scrape the rest of the 
>> script together if I just could find a way to read a specific line.
>> 
>> 
> This pulls the file a line at a time:
>
> for EACH in `cat filenamehere`; do
>   process line as needed
> done

Ordinarily, no it doesn't. $EACH will be each whitespace-separated 
token, not each line. You have two choices if you want to use a shell 
loop:

1. Rely on bash-specific line magic by using a while loop:

    while read L; do echo $L; done < source.txt

2. Reset $IFS to a newline character and do as Russ suggested:

    IFS=$(echo)
    for L in $(<source.txt); do echo $L; done
    unset IFS

Otherwise, Rich's advice about using awk is worth considering!

-- 
Paul Heinlein <> heinlein at madboa.com <> www.madboa.com

The other day as my Dell Dimensions Windows 2000 machine was rebooting 
after another round of patches I was wondering if it was the Microsoft 
patches slowing it down or the Norton updates.  Then I downloaded my 
banking activity into MS-Money and found either my installation was 
hosed or the bank had "tweaked" something as most (but not all) of the 
transactions for the last month duplicated.  About then a pop up window 
appeared.  Norton told me if I didn't pay another year's fee for their 
software which protects me from the software MS sold me then, well, 
awful things might happen and they'd hate to see that happen to a nice 
fellow like me.  They're going to give me 72 hours to come up with the 
dough.

After a few minutes I realized that wasn't my dog growling, it was my 
molars grinding and the old twitch was back in my left eye.  Time to 
move to Linux for my blood pressure I think.

I use Debian stable for a server OS on my network but it can get 
hopelessly outdated for a desktop.  So I'm looking at Knoppix and 
Ubuntu.  I may install both for a while.  Given that I'd like some 
advice on some desktop applications.

E-mail: I confess, I prefer Outlook.  I've been using Thunderbird lately 
and while it's a nice Outlook Express replacement it doesn't have all 
the functionality of Outlook.  I KNOW e-mail clients are a touchy 
subject but what are the favorites out there?  I use POP3, IMAP, Google 
(pop3 + web) and Yahoo (web only) services.  And I've grown accustomed 
to having my calendar and contacts integrated with e-mail.  Tasks, 
notes, and that business of tracking everything I do on the computer I 
DON'T need.

Personal Finance: I use an older version of MS Money right now and it 
keeps me on track.  I've got an accounting background so I can deal with 
complicated accounting systems, but I won't.  If it's simple I'll keep 
up with it.  Again, suggestions?  What works for you?  Ups and downs?

Music: I like Yahoo Launchcast/Music and bought a year's worth in 
advance before the price jumped.  I like it, I don't love it.  It could 
use a broader library (no Hoyt Axon AT ALL???), there are too many 
payola "recommendations" (Going from bluegrass to rap aint workin for 
me) and of course there's no Linux client that I'm aware of.  I've got a 
fair collection of mp3s but I'd like a radio type service that works on 
Linux and can be tailored to my rather eclectic tastes.  Anyone have 
something they really like?

And advice in general is welcome.  This won't be a do or die conversion 
as I've got a new XP laptop to lean on until I'm happy with my Linux 
desktop.

Of course general rants about MS software are ALWAYS appreciated!

John Purser
