line and text-oriented applications. I use pine, others like mutt. I also use
jpilot <http://www.jpilot.org/> as the desktop component of my PDA:
addressbook, calendar, to-do list, notes. I've never cared for integrated
"suites" because they limit choices. You might look at sylpheed and see if
that's closer to what you've come to like and want.

> Personal Finance: I use an older version of MS Money right now and it keeps
> me on track. I've got an accounting background so I can deal with
> complicated accounting systems, but I won't. If it's simple I'll keep up
> with it. Again, suggestions? What works for you? Ups and downs?

   Gnucash <http://www.gnucash.org/>. It ain't difficult, a simplistic
double-entry checkbook register and money management application that
apparently imports Microsoft-generated data. It's great for personal use, but
for business use get SQL-Ledger.

> Music: I like Yahoo Launchcast/Music and bought a year's worth in advance
> before the price jumped. I like it, I don't love it. It could use a broader
> library (no Hoyt Axon AT ALL???), there are too many payola
> "recommendations" (Going from bluegrass to rap aint workin for me) and of
> course there's no Linux client that I'm aware of. I've got a fair
> collection of mp3s but I'd like a radio type service that works on Linux
> and can be tailored to my rather eclectic tastes. Anyone have something
> they really like?

   I've a bookcase stereo system in the office so I've no idea on this.

> And advice in general is welcome.  This won't be a do or die conversion as 
> I've got a new XP laptop to lean on until I'm happy with my Linux desktop.

   OpenOffice.org-2.0 works as an Office replacement. For serious writing, use
LyX, the GUI-front end to LaTeX <http://www.lyx.org/>.

   Even when I was a young curmudgeon I didn't like machines that thought they
were smarter than I am. I still don't like computers to make decisions for
me. Integrated applications fall in this class: you're stuck with what you
get, and you learn that what you're allowed to do is something to emulate as
you defenestrate.

   I believe that one of the most difficult aspects of moving from Microsoft
to linux (or any of the other F/OSS platforms) is coping with choice. You're
moving from a dearth of choices to a plethora of choices. And, they cost you
no money (unless you voluntarily contribute to show appreciation). Don't
think this is a trivial point; it isn't.

   In a thread on 2D vector drawing software I started a couple of days ago, I
noted that I've identified 16 different options, including the venerable
xfig. Others have added to the count. Each is "good" to a different degree,
and each is a bit more capable (or has a shorter learning curve) for
different types of drawings. I've used xfig before moving to tgif, and now
I'm using PSTricks (creating figures directly in PostScript code), jpicedt
(which is a visual creator of PSTricks output), and ipe (which is a visual
editor directly producing .pdf output).

   Writing letters, memos, and other short stuff? There's OpenOffice.org,
AbiWord, Koffice (if you use KDE), a gnome equivalent (I'm sure), LaTeX, text
editors galore, and probably much more of which I am unaware.

   Perhaps it's equivalent to moving from a small city apartment to a rural
house. You go from limited (or no) choices to having options on everything.
It can be scary or overwhelming at first. But, the end result is that you
work with applications that fit *you* and your way of working. Others can
select different applications, yet the data are interchageable (for the most
part) and it doesn't matter what tool created the document.

Have fun and enjoy the journey,

Rich

John Purser wrote:

> I use Debian stable for a server OS on my network but it can get 
> hopelessly outdated for a desktop.  So I'm looking at Knoppix and 
> Ubuntu.  I may install both for a while.  Given that I'd like some 
> advice on some desktop applications.

Maybe Knoppix has improved it's "install to hard disk" since 3.4, but if 
you prefer an "easy to install organization-backed" distro to one that's 
developed and maintained by a closely-knit band of hackers, I'd go with 
Ubuntu if you're looking for Debian compatibility. There are two flavors 
of Ubuntu. Ubuntu itself has a Gnome desktop; Kubuntu has a KDE desktop. 
You could, if you wanted, install Ubuntu and add in KDE from the Debian 
repositories, but I consider that a waste of time and disk space. Pick 
your desktop and pick your Ubuntu.

If you don't want or need Debian compatibility, you could look at 
CentOS. CentOS is an RHEL clone. Other the denizens of this list, there 
isn't a whole lot of Debian adminstration expertise you can fall back on 
if you get into trouble, but there are lots of Red Hat folk. I have one 
system running CentOS 4.2 now, and I like it. It's very stable and 
secure ... the desktop is perhaps a bit "outdated" for your taste, but I 
have had rotten luck with Fedora Core, so I switched to CentOS.

> E-mail: I confess, I prefer Outlook.  I've been using Thunderbird 
> lately and while it's a nice Outlook Express replacement it doesn't 
> have all the functionality of Outlook.  I KNOW e-mail clients are a 
> touchy subject but what are the favorites out there?  I use POP3, 
> IMAP, Google (pop3 + web) and Yahoo (web only) services.  And I've 
> grown accustomed to having my calendar and contacts integrated with 
> e-mail.  Tasks, notes, and that business of tracking everything I do 
> on the computer I DON'T need.

I use Thunderbird and I'm happy with it. I haven't tried the calendar 
piece of the Mozilla suite yet (is it "Sunbird?") Evolution has more 
features and is probably the closest to Outlook in the open source 
world. However ... so-called Personal Information Management software is 
a vibrant field right now in the "web 2.0" space. There are lots of 
gimmicks out there, *especially* if you're willing to trust your data to 
a web-based "service bureau/ASP" model.

Big names like Google are beginning to offer stuff like this, not just 
small teams with advanced ways of managing personal information looking 
for venture capital. I'm much more interested in the second type, 
however, because I *don't* trust my data to a service bureau. :)

Right now I'm tracking something called "Chandler", named after Raymond 
Chandler of the mystery novels. It's about 0.5 release level right now; 
the last release they call stable dates back to about April. I wouldn't 
trust a business to it yet, of course, but it looks like their 
combination of hierarchy (trees), facets and tags has the potential to 
revolutionize the PIM. Have a look at

http://wiki.osafoundation.org/bin/view/Journal/HierarchyVersusFacetsVersusTags
http://wiki.osafoundation.org/bin/view/Journal/TooManyWaysToOrganize
http://wiki.osafoundation.org/bin/view/Journal/ChandlerInFifteenMinutes

and of course

http://del.icio.us/znmeb

> Personal Finance: I use an older version of MS Money right now and it 
> keeps me on track.  I've got an accounting background so I can deal 
> with complicated accounting systems, but I won't.  If it's simple I'll 
> keep up with it.  Again, suggestions?  What works for you?  Ups and 
> downs?

I don't do personal finance in Linux. I think "gnucash" or whatever it's 
called these days is the only thing out there with enough features to 
match Money or Quicken.

> Music: I like Yahoo Launchcast/Music and bought a year's worth in 
> advance before the price jumped.  I like it, I don't love it.  It 
> could use a broader library (no Hoyt Axon AT ALL???), there are too 
> many payola "recommendations" (Going from bluegrass to rap aint workin 
> for me) and of course there's no Linux client that I'm aware of.  I've 
> got a fair collection of mp3s but I'd like a radio type service that 
> works on Linux and can be tailored to my rather eclectic tastes.

My idea of a media player is several hundred classical CDs, noise 
cancelling headphones, and "allclassical.org". Other than that, I can't 
help you. :)

> And advice in general is welcome.  This won't be a do or die 
> conversion as I've got a new XP laptop to lean on until I'm happy with 
> my Linux desktop.

That was actually going to be my *real* advice -- get a new Windows XP 
system with the latest advanced supported hardware and upgrade all your 
applications like a good boy. :) I didn't think that would fly on a 
Linux list, but since *you* mentioned it first ...

<ducking>

> Of course general rants about MS software are ALWAYS appreciated!

I think Microsoft's monopoly on the desktop is fading rather rapidly, 
mostly because of the big money behind Google and the flurry of activity 
in AJAX, web 2.0, Ruby on Rails and the semantic web. As Criswell used 
to say, "The future is where we will spend the rest of our lives".

I'm not sure that a Google monopoly is a better idea than a Microsoft 
monopoly, a Sun monopoly, or an IBM monopoly. And Microsoft certainly 
isn't standing still -- I've heard good things about the UI for the next 
version of Office, and they've got the cash to buy into these emerging 
technologies. Cheap Linux servers, Cheap Linux desktops and cheap 
broadband will move the concentration of power around, but the power 
will still be concentrated *somewhere*. That's the way complex adaptive 
systems work.

Rich Shepard wrote:

> Even when I was a young curmudgeon I didn't like machines that thought 
> they
> were smarter than I am. I still don't like computers to make decisions 
> for
> me. Integrated applications fall in this class: you're stuck with what 
> you
> get, and you learn that what you're allowed to do is something to 
> emulate as
> you defenestrate.

Actually, it's *worse* than that! Because of curmudgeons who want these 
things *their* way, the software limits choice in some ways and provides 
dozens -- hundreds is probably more like it -- of other choices, 
euphemistically referred to as "customizations" in meaningless areas. 
I'm talking about things like how the menus unfold, whether or not you 
have cute sounds or talking paper clips, desktop themes, etc.

Actually, it's worse than *that*! Because a Windows desktop supposedly 
has to serve a family, a small business or a network of knowledge 
workers in an enterprise, it takes lots of time to eliminate the 
distractions built in to the core of it -- media players, surfing the 
web from your desk, instant messaging from people two cubes away, visual 
UIs for auditory thinkers, email arguments preserved for all time. It 
takes a tremendous amount of discipline for a knowledge worker to 
mainain the focus required.

> I believe that one of the most difficult aspects of moving from Microsoft
> to linux (or any of the other F/OSS platforms) is coping with choice. 
> You're
> moving from a dearth of choices to a plethora of choices. And, they 
> cost you
> no money (unless you voluntarily contribute to show appreciation). Don't
> think this is a trivial point; it isn't.

Well ... you can sculpt an elephant two ways. You can start with an 
armature, add clay slowly to it until it has the elephant shape you 
want, or you can start with a big chunk of marble, a hammer and a chisel 
and cut off everything that doesn't look like an elephant. :)

So ... start with Windows XP, Office 200x, Microsoft Money, anti-virus, 
Internet Explorer, etc. and get rid of everything you don't need or use. 
Or start with a bare machine, load Linux, a lightweight desktop like 
XFCE, and add on "best of breed" applications for your tasks.

Either way you'll get close to the elephant you need, and either way it 
*will* be your unique elephant. But the Linux elephant will be less 
expensive. And you'll be able to hang out with folks like us without 
hanging your head in shame. :)

> Writing letters, memos, and other short stuff? There's OpenOffice.org,
> AbiWord, Koffice (if you use KDE), a gnome equivalent (I'm sure), 
> LaTeX, text
> editors galore, and probably much more of which I am unaware.

1. AbiWord is the Gnome word processor, I think.

2. KOffice is pretty lame compared to OpenOffice.org. Flames welcomed 
from KOffice fans. I use the KDE desktop, but I've never done anything 
productive in KOffice.

3. You mentioned LyX for LaTeX work. There is another tool that's 
slightly more advanced but a bit harder to use, called TeXmacs.

On 10/30/05, John Purser <jmpurser at gmail.com> wrote:
> [snip]
> Personal Finance: I use an older version of MS Money right now and it
> keeps me on track.  I've got an accounting background so I can deal with

As a previous (and current) MS Money user I have a few opinions about
the state of open-source personal finances.  An interesting starting
point for now might be the two-part LWN.net article on personal
finance:

http://lwn.net/Articles/149383/
and
http://lwn.net/Articles/153043/

You're not likely to find anything as sleek or smooth working as MS
Money or Quicken, but as far as I know GNUcash is probably the best
choice.  I tried it for a while, got sick of the interface, and went
back to MS Money.  I'll probably try again if they ever get the Gnome
2 port done.  I haven't tried KMyMoney yet, thought it sounds
promising and actually has active developers.

I also experimented briefly with Moneydance!
(http://www.moneydance.com/), which is a closed-source Java-based
personal finance package with a free trial.  It seemed somewhat
impressive, but I never finished playing with it, instead went back to
MS Money (2003).  Personal finances is something I just want to get
done, not spend lots of time on :-)

Jason

P.S.  On a side note, I upgraded at one point to Money 2005 and didn't
like that either, so maybe I'm just too picky.


From mcintoshrt at comcast.net  Sun Oct 30 09:49:13 2005
From: mcintoshrt at comcast.net (Robert McIntosh)
Date: Sun, 30 Oct 2005 09:49:13 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Re: Linux Desktop - Time to make the change
Message-ID: <43650799.8040003@comcast.net>

"I use Debian stable for a server OS on my network but it can get
hopelessly outdated for a desktop.  So I'm looking at Knoppix and
Ubuntu.  I may install both for a while.  Given that I'd like some
advice on some desktop applications."

I highly recommend Mepis Linux (http://www.mepis.org) - I've been using 
it as my Desktop Linux for about 1 year and absolutely love it. 
Hardware installs like a dream (though YMMV).  Everything works with 
minimal configuration, if any.  In my experience, this is unusual for a 
desktop distro with multimedia desires - especially a free one.  Updates 
with apt-get/synaptic run great - Mepis uses some of Debian's 
repositories in addition to it's own.  The install disc is also a 
live-CD so you can try it out to see how well it works with your 
hardware before committing to it.  I've tried both Ubuntu and Knoppix 
and both miss one or two hardware things - nothing major, just didn't 
see the point in spending time with an installer that didn't recognize 
hardware as effortlessly as Mepis.

"E-mail: I confess, I prefer Outlook."

Evolution is a great piece of software.  Provides nearly the same 
functionality as Outlook.  I use Thunderbird because of the better spam 
filtering, though this may have changed in recent versions of Evolution. 
  For calendaring, I use Mozilla's Sunbird, though am highly jealous of 
my wife's iCal on her Mac.  I wish Mozilla would release some worthwhile 
themes for Sunbird/Calendar.

"Personal Finance: I use an older version of MS Money right now"

I'm no accountant and I just keep up with my checking/savings.  I've 
used Moneydance (http://www.moneydance.com)for the last two years 
because of the cross-platform functionality between Windows/Mac/Linux 
(java-based app).  GnuCash may be better for you.

"Of course general rants about MS software are ALWAYS appreciated!"

I do have one rant, and surprisingly it's not directed towards MS: Apple 
needs to release a Linux installer of it's Quicktime software.  This 
genuinely irritates me that they have used BSD's core for their OS and 
then claim to be open source supporters.  Why in the world do they not 
release a Quicktime installer for Linux?  I refuse to buy an iPod on 
this fact alone.  Leaving us non-MS supporters to our own tricks and 
devices is frustrating.  Oh sure, they'll make a Windows installer...

Oh, I haven't abandoned Gentoo as my distro of choice. The only reason 
I'm running CentOS on *one* system is that I'm studying for the RHCE 
exam. Once that's done, that machine gets Gentoo on it again! My main 
workstation and the Linux half of the laptop are still Gentoo. The 
CentOS box is my "learn how to do server stuff" box; when I'm done with 
RHCE it's going to be a Linux/Apache/PostgreSQL/Rails web applications 
platform.

But yes, if you're willing to sacrifice nifty new features for 
rock-solid security, stability and Red Hat compatibility, CentOS 4.2 
makes a *fine* desktop. KDE is 3.3, for example. And so does Sarge.

> Do you use a PalmOS PDA? If so, pilot-link comes with all

I have a Palm PDA (built into an ancient Kyocera cell phone), a Windows 
CE Pocket PC and a Sharp Zaurus. I sync none of them with my desktop or 
each other; they are simply toys as far as I'm concerned. The last 
pocket sized device I actually found useful was the HP 100LX. It had a 
*usable* keyboard, and ran Lotus 1-2-3 2.4 and Derive. I could program 
it in Forth, and it had all the PIM functionality I needed. I have 
*never* found anything as good for the pocket.

The cell phone is about to get replaced with something stripped down 
that just makes phone calls -- no video, no color, no keyboard, no text 
messaging -- nothing but a phone that can remember frequently called 
numbers, of which I have about ten.

mattress? Do you file your taxes electronically, or on paper? Does your 
doctor keep your health records in a database, or on paper? Did you 
write your book with a turkey quill?

   I recently added several postfix restrictions and they all tested OK.
However, now I'm having the problem of a network host not being permitted to
send mail (incoming mail gets to the local user inbox, so it's just
outgoing).

   The MUA on that machine is thunderbird-1.0.6. When we try to send messages
from the draft folder (and there's no "send" icon on the toolbar so we need
to work from the file menu), this message pops up:

"An error occurred while sending mail. THe mail server responded:
<pachy.appl-ecosys.com (192.168.55.5)>: Client host rejected: Access denied.
Please verify that your email address is correct in your Mail preferences and
try again."

   Nothing's changed except for an OS and thunderbird upgrade. The hostname is
in /etc/hosts on the server, so is the IP address. The domain(s) are listed
in /etc/postfix/internal_network.db and /etc/postfix/our_domain_as_sender.db.
The smtp_recipient_restrictions begin with:
         check_client_access hash:/etc/postfix/internal_network
         check_sender_access hash:/etc/postfix/not_our_domain_as_sender
         reject_non_fqdn_recipient
         reject_non_fqdn_sender
         reject_unknown_sender_domain
         reject_unknown_recipient_domain
         permit_mynetworks

   I just do not see where the error is that prevents outgoing mail from
pachy. I'd appreciate your ideas -- and solutions.

From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Sun Oct 30 14:04:50 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Sun, 30 Oct 2005 14:04:50 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Subject: A New MTA Glitch -- UNHITCHED
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510301401210.11787@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

   Is this a great mail list, or what? As soon as I sent the original message
I looked again at /etc/postfix/main.cf and that prompted me to look again at
/etc/postfix/our_domain_as_sender. Sure enough, I had only one domain name
there, not both of 'em.

   Added the other name, ran postmap, reloaded postfix and the problem's
solved.

   I think the act of asking for help provide the mental equivalent of a does
of Epsom(R) salts: it clears away the clog and makes the brain squeaky clean
once again.

   Thanks, PLUG!

RS> I believe that one of the most difficult aspects of moving from
RS> Microsoft to linux (or any of the other F/OSS platforms) is coping
RS> with choice. You're moving from a dearth of choices to a plethora
RS> of choices. And, they cost you no money (unless you voluntarily
RS> contribute to show appreciation). Don't think this is a trivial
RS> point; it isn't.

sorry but to me this is either wrong or just too simple.

I have tried (and am trying again) to move from MS to Linux.  I
program for a living.  Luckily for that the sw that i use now has a
port to Linux so it makes that much easier.

But to intimate that Linux has a 'plethora' of options and MS doesn't
just isn't right.  Cost is important .. but it isn't the bottom line.
(i love it when linux guys always through that in<G>).  I do like to
donate ..

For myself (and a couple other guys i know that hare trying or have
tried) the real problem is that SW doesn't come complete.  It may be a
thrill and a hoot to have to go out an look for bits and pieces that
come up as missing when you try to install something.  But for me it
is a waste of time.  I have just loaded Fedora 4 on my laptop (it is
my main workstation.  If i can't get it to work with all hw on it then
i can't use it).  I loaded FULL as i really didn't want to see any of
those 'can't do that cause of missing bits' messages.  But I needed a
DVD player so I did a search on google and found one and downloaded
and tried to install.  Opps .. this library isn't there .. that bit
isn't there.  Did another search for some of the bits and there was no
clear information about the packages I needed.

THIS is the biggest problem.  You are right is saying there are a
'plethora' of options, though i believe generally there are still more
in MS, but the options often seem to be incomplete.  Well unless you
simply want a command line text editor.

I know that lots of linux guys don't like the gui .. lots (well the
ones i have talked to) have no problems with going out and finding the
pieces that didn't come through the install and other <shudder> can't
wait for a quick compile.

But lots of us who would love to make the move find it too time
consuming to do things .. like install a program.

By the way .. i love most all the bits you put up here .. smart and
insightful.  This just bugged me cause i had a bad weekend trying to
install linux apps <G>

DSig

You're absolutely right ... a Linux desktop is more work than a Windows 
desktop in the current state of things. However:

1. I recommend against Fedora Core for desktops. Debian-based systems 
have a better package management system and lots more packages 
available. Gentoo is close, but you might find having to recompile 
things too much trouble, and the Gentoo installer is definitely a 
hindrance for folks used to Windows. Of course, I also recommend against 
Fedora Core for servers; Debian Sarge and CentOS are my choices there.

2. Media players and digital media are an area littered with 
intellectual property issues. Some very rich folks want to keep you from 
ripping them off, so don't expect a lot of help in this area.

From jmpurser at gmail.com  Mon Oct 31 09:05:46 2005
From: jmpurser at gmail.com (John Purser)
Date: Mon, 31 Oct 2005 09:05:46 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Desktop - Time to make the change - Update
Message-ID: <479803a70510310905k18db4f6an272eb944d2531bb8@mail.gmail.com>

Thanks to all who gave me the benefit of your experience.  I now have
a triple boot Dell that runs Windows 2000, Ubuntu, and Kubuntu.  The
new Debian  installer quit instantly when booting from the CD so I'll
take a look at installing Debian from *ubuntu after I've read the
applicable section of the installation manual.

The *ubuntus are SLICK on the install.  Very nice.  The only problem
I've had so far was Ubuntu wouldn't burn a CD for me.  It kept saying
there was no media in the drive.

After the cautionary advice about Knoppix on this list I checked their
web page and sure enough they caution that some of the choices they
made to create a kick ass (it really is!) live distribution on CD make
it less than desireable as a hard drive install.  So I skipped it. 
I'll still keep a CD around for rescues though.

I'm installing these systems in 10 gig partitions on a 60 gig disk
with a shared swap partition.  No problem with hardware detection
(Dell Dimensions, Pentium 850, 256 Megs ram, dual hard drives, dual
CDs) or networking (DHCP, router, cable modem).  It all just works. 
Lovely!

I plan on installing a few more distributions and then spending a
couple of weekends playing with them.  Grub makes multi booting so
handy I think I'll keep one extra install for experiments.

On converting from MS I think some valid points were made on both
sides of the "choice" debate.  Linux does have a lot of options and of
course I'm looking for the one that's exactly the same as what I've
always used!  I'm looking for a revolution that doesn't actually
CHANGE anything.  Not bright!

This is just FUN!  Thanks again for the help.

John Purser


From rshepard at appl-ecosys.com  Mon Oct 31 09:06:06 2005
From: rshepard at appl-ecosys.com (Rich Shepard)
Date: Mon, 31 Oct 2005 09:06:06 -0800 (PST)
Subject: [PLUG] Linux Desktop - Time to make the change
Message-ID: <Pine.LNX.4.63.0510310905470.24810@salmo.appl-ecosys.com>

On Mon, 31 Oct 2005, David Tod Sigafoos wrote:

> For myself (and a couple other guys i know that hare trying or have tried)
> the real problem is that SW doesn't come complete. It may be a thrill and a
> hoot to have to go out an look for bits and pieces that come up as missing
> when you try to install something. But for me it is a waste of time. I have
> just loaded Fedora 4 on my laptop (it is my main workstation. If i can't
> get it to work with all hw on it then i can't use it). I loaded FULL as i
> really didn't want to see any of those 'can't do that cause of missing
> bits' messages. But I needed a DVD player so I did a search on google and
> found one and downloaded and tried to install. Opps .. this library isn't
> there .. that bit isn't there. Did another search for some of the bits and
> there was no clear information about the packages I needed.

David,

   This is a real problem, but one that's easily solved. Really! Let me try to
address some solutions.

   First, your distribution of choice. I went from DOS to linux in 1997 (with
a small window thrown in because I had no choice for some software) and used
Red Hat 4.0 because a clerk at Powell's suggested that over Slackware. From
then through 7.3 I went through dependency hell with every upgrade
(espefially the major number ones) and adding new apps. A couple of years ago
I moved from Red Hat 7.3 to Slackware-8.1 and haven't looked back since.
Other than security patches I do nothing from one release to the next. When I
want additional software, I grab the tarball and build it here. The only
problem I found was with source that wants the C++ STL; apparently Pat
doesn't like it and doesn't include it with Slackware. It's a known problem
and application providers have a work-around that solves the problem.

   My point is that Red Hat is not the only choice. I understand that Mepsis
is outstanding at recognizing hardware and including the proper drivers. A
lot of folks like Debian and its derivatives and feel they need to apt-get
every night. Shrug. They're welcome to it. There are probably more than five
dozen distributions available; you'll easily find one that works for you.
SuSE-10.0 is also supposed to be super simple and recognizes everthing. I've
read good things about Mandriva (nee Mandrake) over the years. And the list
goes on.

> I know that lots of linux guys don't like the gui .. lots (well the ones i
> have talked to) have no problems with going out and finding the pieces that
> didn't come through the install and other <shudder> can't wait for a quick
> compile.

   I started working on mainframes in the early 1960s, was away from computers
for about a decade, and returned in the early 1970s. No GUIs, no full screen
editors. Paper tape, boxes of 80-column Hollerith cards that we dropped on
the floor, Teletype terminals. So, yes, I prefer the command line. But, I
really like working in X because I have 10 virtual desktops, can cut and
paste data from one application to another and prefer text-based applications
in virtual terminals. That written, I've become addicted to LyX, the GUI
frontend to LaTeX because I can write so much quicker than with emacs or
another text editor.

> But lots of us who would love to make the move find it too time consuming
> to do things .. like install a program.

   You'll make up the time in not rebooting. :-)

   And, once you have the set of applications you need and want, there's
nothing to do by use them.

> By the way .. i love most all the bits you put up here .. smart and
> insightful. This just bugged me cause i had a bad weekend trying to install
> linux apps <G>

   Thanks for the compliment. And, when you have frustrations with an
installation, write to the list. It works.

Keep us informed of progress,

Rich

distributions cause many issues.

On Mon, 31 Oct 2005, David Tod Sigafoos wrote:

> And this leads to another BIG problem .. which distribution.  You as 10
> people you will get 8 different answers .. each may have 'linux' as the OS
> but linux is useless without something around it.

    I think it's that you ask 8 people and get 10 different answers.

> Each distribution has its own packages and libraries and capabilities. I
> never would have thought that resolving missing bits depended on the
> distribution .. oh my. A package is a package .. and should be complete.

    That's true. But, it's how the distribution handles the packages that
changes.

    Some distributions aim toward business users, others toward home or casual
users, still others toward the dedicated geek. Since we don't know what you
need or want, we can only suggest. It's your choice. Or, as John is doing,
install a bunch and see which you like.

    Then, of course, you have to decide on how to manage X. You want a
Microsoft-like appearance? Try KDE or Gnome. Want something simpler? Try
Xfce. Want just window managers? Try ..., well, there are just so many!

> Linux as a os may be great (and i am sure from my readings and study that
> it is MUCH better than Windows) but the Heinz 47 flavours of distributions
> cause many issues.

    See? This is what I meant about too many choices. :-) When you find one you
like, life will return to simplicity. But, don't expect this to occur
shortly.

> This is another big problem.  IF Linux can't do all the things that windows
> does then why should one change?

    Perhaps you shouldn't. Many of us don't like the things that Winduhs does.
You can play DVDs to your heart's content with xmms, xine, and a bunch of
other stuff.

Just one recommendation. 
I recently tried the latest release of UBUNTU and I have to say.... WOW!
This dist is the first viable linux desktop I have seen. 
The advantages for newcomers to linux are:
1) The synaptic package manager. It makes it easy to search and install
almost any package. And when I say almost any package I mean it. I
expected to have to build alot of apps from source, but to my surprise
and delight they were in the repos at ubuntu. I was always a big debian
fan but hated using apt(Probably because I never took the time to learn
it.). I liked the ease of use with Yum on Fedora. Being able to use
wildcards when installing etc. But synaptic makes my life much easier...
2) Its Debian based! Havent had any problems yet with installing debian
packages. And of course it has the stability and reliability of Debian! 
3) Minimalist installation. Why open yourself to trouble until you
really need to? Ubuntu installs a very bare minimum install and the
server install is even more trim. I love it! However the fact that the
firewall is installed but not running off the get go is a little
troubling. Sound, but troubling. Novices can install firestarter or the
likes. others can throw in their favorite iptables script. (one note..
the 'recent' iptables module was misplaced in debian unstable for a
while and was put back in. This means that currently ubuntu is missing
the 'recent' module but it will be back in soon per their bug list...)

I would recommend trying this distro if you are having growing pains
with others... It makes converting alot easier!!!
 
Hope this helps...
JM

Jason,

Sorry, I wasn't looking to spread FUD. But I cannot compile my kernel (unbder
/usr/src/linux) on RH ES 4 EL WITH the kernel dev package installed (That
replaced .srcs.rpm from rh 3). I simply want to compile the kernel with AGPGART
as a module instead of having it built-in. I can't even do a make modules
without an error. I know there is some other way to build modules, since back
in August I was able to do it. Of course I didn't write it down...

I can also tell you, as I work for a major software vendor who has
proprietary (non-GPL) source in the rh kernel, that redhat does have some
proprietary source in their kernel. I'm not necessarily sure that's the resaon
you can't just go into the kernel tree and run make though. I imagine it has
more to do with not wanting to support every homegrown kernel between here and
Gentoo. But since this is my laptop, I'm not worried about being supported.

After further investigatiopn it appears that I may need a kernel tree under
/usr/src/redhat/BUILD, which is where I probably did the make modules. So, like
Steve said, it's not broken...

Dan H.

From ianburrell at gmail.com  Mon Oct 31 13:54:42 2005
From: ianburrell at gmail.com (Ian Burrell)
Date: Mon, 31 Oct 2005 13:54:42 -0800
Subject: [PLUG] Redhat ES 4
In-Reply-To: <1130786697.43666f8a006ea@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
References: <1130541696.4362b2806bda8@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
	<ff01ea180510282256q55d7fd7br2d98bc0aafbead0d@mail.gmail.com>
	<c29537840510291055i641f0b4cyd6e6adb98466e9f9@mail.gmail.com>
	<1130786697.43666f8a006ea@webmail.fiddlers-green.info>
Message-ID: <d91f09cd0510311354o6e3cb3dai3ea672bdb86e1910@mail.gmail.com>

On 10/31/05, dan at fiddlers-green.info <dan at fiddlers-green.info> wrote:
>
> Sorry, I wasn't looking to spread FUD. But I cannot compile my kernel (unbder
> /usr/src/linux) on RH ES 4 EL WITH the kernel dev package installed (That
> replaced .srcs.rpm from rh 3). I simply want to compile the kernel with AGPGART
> as a module instead of having it built-in. I can't even do a make modules
> without an error. I know there is some other way to build modules, since back
> in August I was able to do it. Of course I didn't write it down...
>

That is because kernel-devel does not replace the kernel src.rpm.  It
replaces kernel-source for building third-party modules.  Redhat
basically decided that most users don't need to 100 MB kernel source
while 13 MB of headers is sufficient for building third-party modules.

The complete source used to build the kernel rpm's is in the src.rpm. 
It has the kernel tarball   To build you own kernel manually, you can
do 'rpmbuild -bp' which will unpack the tarball and apply the patches.
 Then you can do into the /usr/src/rpm/BUILD directory and see the
unpacked sources.

> I can also tell you, as I work for a major software vendor who has
> proprietary (non-GPL) source in the rh kernel, that redhat does have some
> proprietary source in their kernel. I'm not necessarily sure that's the resaon
> you can't just go into the kernel tree and run make though. I imagine it has
> more to do with not wanting to support every homegrown kernel between here and
> Gentoo. But since this is my laptop, I'm not worried about being supported.
>

This is false.  All the sources for the redhat kernel is in the
src.rpm.  In fact, it is available to anyone on ftp.redhat.com.  The
basis is a kernel 2.6.9 tarball.  As far as everyone else is aware,
all the patches are GPL.  The src.rpm builds the binary kernel.  In
fact, Centos and others use the src.rpm to build their distributions.

 - Ian

the default kernel won't install the OS, then its time to look for
another.

FWIW, I've had more success with Debian than any other installer, but
only because the Debian installer allows you to take control of just
about every aspect of the installation process.  This installer is not
good for newbies, but the defaults should get most systems up and
running.

From romanmir_ug at comcast.net  Mon Oct 31 20:51:02 2005

Subject: [PLUG] grub vs. lilo
Message-ID: <4366F436.6090009@comcast.net>

Greetings to all on a fine Halloween.

    Ever since Mandrake 8, I have been running lilo as a bootloader. It 
was the default for a Mandrake install. It worked, so I left it alone. I 
ran across a cool command-line switch that helped me boot to winders 
whenever I wanted to do some "A list" gaming.

    Then, about two months ago, I loaded Ubuntu-linux and was caught a 
little off guard by it's insistence for installing grub. Naturally, my 
nifty little windows reboot script broke. "Oh well," I thought, "I bet 
there is a way of doing this in grub."

    Alas, my chagrin has been extended by my inability to find a way to 
emulate the "-R" switch for lilo in grub.

    I haven't been able to find anything in the Ubuntu forums of any 
great use. I have tried many suggestions that they have had. All to no 
avail.

    Even google hasn't been it's usual useful self.

Is there a way to be able to 
"simply-hit-a-button-on-the-desktop-and-boot-into-windows" using grub?

can theoretically just compile the module and load it - it's almost as 
easy as using a prepackaged driver (BTW, when places start releasing

it is on Windows).

> DL> good for newbies, but the defaults should get most systems up and
> DL> running.
> 
> When you grap an XP disk or server 2003 disk .. you stuff it in ..
> press the button and off you go.  Oh yeah .. you have to know what
> region you are in but other than that  .. BANG BIFF POW you have a
> system that knows EVERYTHING about your hardware.
> 
> I love to hear the 'recompile kernel' bit <G>
